Homer  C.  Davenport 


America's  National  Game 
By  A.  G.  SPALDING 

Price,  $2.00  Net 

A  book  of  600  pages,  profusely  illustrate<i 
with  over  100  full  page  engravings,  and  hav- 
ing sixteen  forceful  cartoons  by  Homer  C. 
Davenport,  the  famous  American  artist. 

No  man  in  America  is  better  equipped 
\o  write  on  all  the  varied  phases  of  the  Na- 
tional Game  than  is  A.  G.  Spalding.  His 
observation  and  experience  began  when  the 
game  was  young.  He  gained  fame  as  a 
pitcher  forty  years  ago,  winning  a  record  as 
player  that  has  never  yet  been  equalled. 
He  was  associated  with  the  management  of 
the  pastime  through  trying  years  of  struggle 
against  prevailing  evils.  He  opposed  the 
gamblers;  he  fought  to  eradicate 
drunkenness  ;  he  urged  and  intro- 
duced new  and  higher  ideals  for 
the  sport ;  he  was  quick  to  see  that 
ball  playbg  and  the  business  man- 
agement of  clubs,  at  the  same  time 
and  by  the  same  men,  were  imprac- 
ticable ;  he  knew  that  ball  players 
might  be  quite  competent  as  magnates,  but  not  while  playing 
the  game ;  he  was  in  the  forefront  of  the  fight  against  syndi- 
cating Base  Ball  and  making  of  a  Nation's  pastime  a  sordid 
Trust;  he  was  the  pioneer  to  lead  competing  American 
Base  Ball  teams  to  a  foreign  land  ;  he  took  two  champion 
teams  to  Great  Britain  in  I  874,  and  two  others  on  a  tour  of 
the  world  in  1  888-9 ;  he  was  present  at  the  birth  of  the 
National  League,  and  has  done  as  much  as  any  living 
American  to  uphold  and  prolong  the  life  of  this  great  pioneer 
Base  Ball  organization. 

When  A.  G.  Spalding   talks  about  America's  National 
Game  he  speaks  by  authority  of  that  he  does  know,  because 


he  has  been  in  the  councils  of  the  management  whenever 
there  have  been  times  of  strenuous  endeavor  to  purge  it 
from  abuses  and  keep  it  clean  for  the  people  of  America 
— young  and  old 

In  this  work  Mr.  Spalding,  after  explaining  the  causes 
that  led  him  into  the  undertaking,  begins  with  the  inception 
of  the  sport ;  shows  how  it  developed,  by  natural  stages 
from  a  boy  with  a  ball  to  eighteen  men,  ball,  bats  and  bases ; 
gives  credit  for  the  first  scientific  application  of  system  to 
the  playing  of  the  game  to  Abner  Doubleday,  of  Coopers- 
town,  N.  Y.:  treats  of  the  first  Base  Ball  club  ;  shows  how 
rowdyism  terrorized  the  sport  in  its  early  days ;  how  gam- 
bling and  drunkenness  brought  the  pastime  into  disfavor 
with  the  masses,  and  how  early  organizations  were  unable 
to  control  the  evils  that  insidiously  crept  in.  He  then 
draws  a  series  of  very  forceful  pictures  of  the  struggle  to 
eradicate  gambling,  drunkenness  and  kindred  evils,  and  shows 
how  the  efforts  of  strcmg  men  accomplished  the  salvation  of 
the  great  American  game  and  placed  it  in  the  position  it  occu- 
pies to-day — the  most  popular  outdoor  pastime  in  the  world. 

Interspersed  throughout  this  interesting  book  are  remin- 
iscences of  Mr.  Spalding's  own  personal  observations  and 
experiences  in  the  game  as  player,  manager  and  magnate, 
covering  a  period  of  many  years.  Some  of  these  stories 
deal  with  events  of  great  import  to  Base  Ball,  and  others 
have  to  do  with  personal  acts  and  characteristics  of  players 
prominent  in  the  game  in  earlier  days — old  time  favorites 
like  Harry  and  George  Wright,  A.  C.  Anson,  Mike  Kelly, 
Billy  Sunday  and  others. 

This  book  should  be  in  the  library  of  every  father  in 
the  land,  for  it  shows  how  his  boy  may  be  built  up  physically 
and  morally  through  a  high-class  pastime.  It  should  be  in 
the  hands  of  every  lad  in  America,  for  it  demonstrates  the 
possibilities  to  American  youth  of  rising  to  heights  of  eminent 
material  success  through  a  determined  adherence  to  things 
that  make  for  the  upbuilding  of  character  in  organizations  as 
well  as  of  men. 

Mailed  postpaid  on  receipt  of  price  by  any  Spalding  store 
(see  list  on  inside  front  cover),  or  by  the  publishers, 

American  Sports  Publishing  Company 

21  Warren  Street,  New  York

SPALDING'S    LAWN    TENNIS    ANNUAL. 


American   Championship 

As  has  been  the  case  for  many  years  in  the  National  Championship  event, 
the  dominant,  overpowering  ability  of  Champion  William  A.  Larned  was 
again  the  principal  factor  of  the  day,  and  for  the  seventh  time  America's 
premier  honors  fell  into  his  accustomed  grasp.  What  with  all  the  gossip 
of  the  unexcelled  condition  of  some  of  the  veterans  and  ex-champions  and 
the  marvellous  leaps  of  improvement  shown  by  the  younger  element  of 
"comers,"  there  were  those  who  hinted  expressions  of  forebodings  and 
regrets  for  what  might  happen  to  the  redoubtable  "Bill,"  but  it  was  not  to 
be.  It  was  again  the  teacher  with  his  pupils,  clay  in  the  hands  of  the 
potter,  and  to  McLoughlin  fell  the  role  of  pupil  for  the  demonstration.  It 
was  the  reac^-haired  youth  from  California  who  earned  an  unquestionable 
right,  by  virtue  of  of  genuine  superiority,  to  try  to  wrest  from  Larned  the 
laurels  so  lom*  and  ably  worn.  And  it  was  he  who  suffered  a  defeat  for 
the  audacious  boldness  of  his  play,  which  proved  beyond  a  doubt  that 
Larned  stands  \i  America  without  an  equal.  The  challenge  match  was  one- 
sided, uneven,  more  so  than  many  of  the  matches  which  McLoughlin  won 
on  his  way  up  the  ladder  from  several  able  opponents.  On  the  whole  the 
tournament,  which  began  August  21,  resulted  very  much  as  had  been 
expected,  with  the  possible  exception  of  the  doubles  championship,  in  which 
final  match  Hackett  and  Alexander,  four  times  national  title-holders,  yielded 
before  the  attack  of  their  challengers,  Little  and  Touchard,  after  a  fast, 
«;ven  battle  lasting  through  four  sets. 

Each  season  for  several  years  past  has  established  a  record  for  the  num- 
ber of  entrants,  and  191 1  proved  no  exception.  Two  hundred  and  two 
enrolled  against  one  hundred  and  eighty  the  year  before.  There  was,  as 
usual,  a  large  number  of  defaults,  but  not  so  many  as  on  several  past  occa- 
sions. Four  days  of  rain  toward  the  close  of  the  tournament  hindered  and 
delayc^  matches  greatly,  and  it  was  not  until  two  weeks  had  elapsed  that 
the  event  came  to  an  end. 

The  draw  was  unusually  well  divided,  and  in  each  eight  there  was  at 
least  o»ie  competitor  belonging  m  the  first  class. 

McLoughlin's  triumphal  march  was  the  result  of  a  brand  of  tennis  of 
which  there  must  be  but  little  criticism  and  unbounded  praise.  Never 
before  had  he  displayed  such  consistency  or  sustained  excellence  of  play. 
Not  once  was  he  in  danger  and,  until  he  encountered  B.  C.  Wright  in  the 
final,  he  did  not  lose  a  set. 

In  spite  of  the  number  of  matches  the  tournament  was  run  off  in  splen- 
did shape,  characterized  by  a  businesslike  precision,  and  with  really  no 
unnecessary  delays.  The  inclement  weather  at  its  wind-up  was  the  only 
feature  to  mar  proceedings  and  the  improvement  was  remarked  on  all  sides. 

Only  two  matches  worthy  of  attention  resulted  from  meetings  in  the 
preliminary  round.  One,  the  base  line  exchanges  between  K.  H.  Behr  and 
the  veteran-  R.  Stevens,  was  interesting  and  a  really  excellent  example  of 
that  passe  style  of  play.  Behr  had  the  better  of  it  and  won  in  four  fairly 
even  sets.  The  other  match  was  the  fight  between  T.  C.  Bundy  and  F.  C. 
Inman.  The  New  Yorker  started  like  a  race  horse,  but  soon  played  him- 
self out,  after  which  Bundy's  relentless,  even  attacks  dominated  the  situa- 
tion completely. 

The  first   round  was  much  more  interesting.     E.   P.    Larned,  quite   unex 

fectedly,  met  his  Waterloo  in  the  person  of  A.  S.   Dabney.    The  champion's 
rother    had    one     of    his    too    numerous    streaky    days,    and     after    barely 
holding  his  own  for  a  time,   went  to   pieces  badly  in  the  fifth   set. 

W.  M.  Washburn  outplayed  A.  S.  Cragin  easily,  winning  in  straight  sets, 
and  Karl  Behr  made  short  work  of  R.  P.   Schwengers.     The  big  Victorian 


6  SPALDING'S    LAWN    TENNIS    ANNUAL. 

was  a  decided  disappointment,  for  although  unable' to  round  into  shape  in 
earlier  events,  better  results  were  excepted  of  him  when  he  reached 
Newport. 

Nothing  startling  occurred  in  the  next  round.  F.  H.  Gates,  Yale's  ablest 
player,  caught  Touchard  when  the  latter  was  having  a  hard  time  with  him- 
self and  made  it  exciting  enough  for  a  time  by  leading  at  two  sets  to  one. 
E.  H.  Whitney  lacked  speed  to  such  an  extent  that  Dean  Mathey  did  as 
he  chose  with  his  returns,  winning  at  2/6,  8/6,  7/5,  7/5.  Whitney's  game 
was  the  better  that  day  in  every  respect,  save  space,  of  which  his  Princeton 
adversary  had  aplenty. 

It  was  the  fourth  round  that  marked  the  beginning  of  many  good 
matches.  Hall  and  Behr  clashed,  but  the  old  Yale  leader  had  too  much 
pace  and  energy  for  Hall,  and  after  dividing  the  first  two  sets  ran  out  the 
third  and  fourth  with  no  trouble  at  all.  Craig  Eiddle  was  in  erratic  mood. 
Although  steady,  the  conservative  efforts  of  J.  D.  S.  Jones  were  too  much 
to  successfully  combat.  C.  M.  Bull,  Jr.,  got  out  of  a  deep  hole  into  which 
the  accurate  shots  of  W.  _F.  Johnson  had  put  him,  and  after  being  within 
a  point  of  defeat  rallied  nicely.  The  fifth  set  was  a  walkover.  Bull  improv- 
ing steadily  and  cutting  off  his  opponent's  slices  with  excellent  volleying. 

In  the  fifth  round  J.  O.  Ames  came  within  an  ace  of  eliminating  the 
veteran  R.  H.  Palmer.  Palmer  was  wild  as  a  hawk  until  within  a  point  or 
two  of  defeat,  when  he  suddenly  got  his  famous  left  arm  going.  There- 
after, Ames  stopped  a  few  and  took  what  came  his  way  in  the  shape  of 
his  rival's  errors,  but  the  result  could  be  but  one  way,  the  deciding  set 
going  to  Palmer  at  6/2.  A  match,  which  many  who  saw  the  tournament 
through  declared  to  have  been  the  best  of  the  week,  was  one  between 
those  old  rivals,  Behr  and  Little.  From  every  standpoint  it  stood  out 
prominently.  The  tennis  played  was  excellent,  and  until  the  last  ball 
crossed  the  net  the  result  hung  in  the  balance.  Weather,  court  conditions 
and  the  forrn  of  the  men  might  have  been  accepted  as  a  forerunner  of 
what  was  to  happen.  Both  were  at  their  best,  and  that  fact  of  itself  tells 
the  story. 

Little  started  off  with  a  rush  that  left  Behr  far  behind.  His  driving  was 
perfect  and  he  passed  Behr,  who  ran  in  on  everything  with  astonishing 
ease;  6/0  was  the  score  at  the  end  of  sixteen  minutes*  play.  The  second 
set  was  better.  Behr  improved  with  every  stroke  and  eighteen  games  were 
necessary  before  Little  annexed  it.  Still  playing  faultlessly.  Little  got  to 
5/3  and  40/15.  With  his  defeat  an  apparent  certainty,  Behr  raced  in  and 
by  several  beautiful  volleys  of  fast  drives  pulled  up  to  five-all.  Thereafter 
Little  was  within  a  point  of  winning  the  set  three  times,  but  eventually 
Behr  took  it  at  8/6.  Encouraged  by  his  recovery,  Behr  started  at  an  even 
faster  clip,  and  the  honors  of  the  fourth  set  were  his,  six  games  to  one. 
The  fifth  was  a  fight  from  beginning  to  end.  At  4/3  Little  won  a  game 
and  took  the  ninth  after  Behr  had  got  to  40/15.  The  tenth  was  over 
quickly.  Little  drove  by  his  opponent  three  times  for  aces  and  a  net  ended 
the  match. 

A.  S.  Dabney  failed  to  give  McLoughlin  much  of  a  fight.  His  usually 
clever  ground  strokes  were  hopelessly  disabled  by  the  Californian's  light- 
ning service,  and  at  other  angles  of  the  game  he  was  outclassed.  The 
second  best  match  of  the  round  was  the  battle  on  the  championship  court 
between  Niles  and  Long.  Niles  was  at  the  very  top  of  his  game,  but  the 
same  cannot  be  said  for  Long.  He  lacked  fire  and  dash  and  apparently 
was  without  the  usual  fighting  spirit  he  generally  has.  He  was  quite  list- 
less, and  when  it  became  obvious  to  Niles  that  his  rival  was  going  to  play 
back,  not  at  the  net,  he  did  likewise  and  defeated  him  by  a  good  margin. 
Long  got  the  third  set  by  coming  in,  but  in  the  fourth  he  gave  up  the 
net  and  was  badly  defeated  in  a  rather  tame  exchange  of  ground  strokes. 

The  biggest  surprise  of  the  tournament  came  in  the  next  round.  Rain 
the  night  before  had  rendered  some  of  the  courts  almost  unplayable  and 
all    so   muddy   and   soft   that   a   fast   game  was   out   of   the   question.     The 


SPALDING'S    LAWN    TENNIS    ANNUAL.  7 

round  brought  together  W.  M.  Washburn,  the  sixteen-year-old  New  York 
schoolboy,  and  Little,  and  it  was  a  foregone  conclusion  that  Washburn 
would  have  all  the  pleasures  of  an  ordinary  spectator  from  that  time   on. 

Little  started  well  and  for  five  games  his  youthful  rival  did  little  more 
than  score  an  occasional  point,  but  at  5/0  a  change  came  over  him  and  he 
scored  game  after  game  till  he  had  pulled  up  level.  Even  then  Little  did 
not  apparently  realize  his  danger,  and  at  seven-all  Washburn  broke  through 
for  the  set.  That  evidently  caused  his  rival  to  consider  more  thoughtfully 
the  possibilities  of  what  might  happen  and  for  two  sets  Little  had  a  decided 
advantage.  He  again  eased  off,  however,  and  Washburn  took  an  early 
lead  which  he  maintained  till  the  score  stood  at   two  sets-all. 

Determined  to  end  it  now.  Little  got  off  quickly  and  soon  had  the  score 
at  3/1  in  his  favor,  but  at  that  point  Washburn  began  a  streak  of  fast,  low 
driving  against  which  his  opponent's  frantic  efforts  went  for  naught.  Five 
straight  games  reeled  off  gave  him  the  set  and  match,  and  a  notch  on  his 
racket  for  his  first  big  scalp. 

It  is  true  that  Little  was  handicapped  by  the  low  bounding  balls  and 
slippery  court  surface  and  that  the  same  conditions  suited  Washburn  per- 
fectly, but  his  careless  beginning  lost  him  the  match,  and  he  had  only 
himself  to  blame.  Washburn  is  by  far  the  most  promising  young  star  the 
East  has  developed  for  many  seasons  and  much  may  be  expected  of  him 
in  the  future.  An  ideal  temperament,  height,  strength  and  natural  ability 
are  all   his,  and  the  remaining  necessity,  experience,   is  bound   to   come. 

Floundering  about  on  the  adjoining  court,  T.  C.  Bundy  was  having  no 
end  of  trouble  with  another  future  star,  G.  M.  Church.  By  better  net  play 
the  Californian  got  off  with  the  first  set,  8/6,  but  Church  had  it  all  his 
own  way  in  the  second,  winning  at  6/3.  The  third  set  was  nip  and  tuck 
up  to  six-all,  when  Church,  by  really  splendid  smashing  and,  under  the 
circumstances,  par  driving,  took  the  necessary  two  games  to  win.  Bundy 
came  back  strong  in  the  fourth,  losing  but  one  of  the  seven  games  played, 
but  Church  was  not  yet  through,  and  at  four-all  broke  through  Bundy's 
delivery.  Here  was  the  young  star's  chance,  but  he  let  it  pass.  Four 
times  he  had  to  resort  to  a  second  service  and  four  times  he  was  passed 
clean  as  he  foolishly  tried  to  run  in.  Bundy  was  quick  to  press  his  advan- 
tage,  and   the   match   ended  with  the   two  games  that   followed, 

C.  R.  Gardner  passed  Gross  for  the  round  ahead  and  R.  H.  Palmer  was 
outclassed  by  Touchard.  McLoughlin  eliminated  G.  P.  Gardner.  The 
other  winners  were  Wright,  Niles  and  Jones,  none  of  whom  were  hard 
pressed.  . 

In  the  top  half  Touchard  earned  a  semi-final  bracket  by  defeating  C.  R. 
Gardner  in  four  tame  sets.  The  loser  played  half-heartedly,  and  thou.c;h 
he  won  the  third  set  was  never  a  serious  contender.  Against  McLoughlin 
Washburn  was  helpless.  Standing  way  behind  the  base  line  to  receive  the 
Westerner's  service,  he  found  the  latter  at  the  net  waiting  for  his  returns, 
which  were  promptly  accepted  and  a  point  v.-as  a  result.  Without  a  chance 
of  breaking  through,  and  having  dropped  his  own  service  several  times, 
the  youngster  seemed  to  realize  the  uselessness  of  his  attempts,  and  before 
the  match  was  half  over  began  a  listless  finish,  which  indicated  plainly  his 
belief   that  defeat   was   inevitable. 

In  the  lower  section,  Bundy  made  short  work  of  Jones,  but  Wright  had 
as  narrow  as  any  of  the  innumerable  escapes  he  has  had  in  his  long  career. 
The  man  who  deferred  a  defeat  for  the  Bostonian  was  Niles,  and  to  two 
bad  blunders  alone  can  he  attribute  his  failure  to  convert  it  into  a  victory. 
For  two  sets  Niles  had  all  the  better  of  it;  then  Wright  forged  to  the 
front.  The  fourth  was  the  turning  point.  Wright  got  to  3/0,  4/1  and  5/3, 
but  Niles  put  on  steam  and  soon  made  it  five-all.  A  little  later  it  was  y/S 
and  40/30  for  Niles.  but  a  wretched  smash  of  a  short  lob  made  it  deuce. 
An  identical  opportunity  came  two  points  later  and  Niles  repeated  his 
former  mistake  in  precisely  the  same  manner.  It  was  the  best  chance  he 
had,   for  thereafter  Wright   led  all   the   way.     The  fifth  set  was   much  like 


SPALDING'S    LAWN    TENNIS    ANNUAL.  9 

the  fourth.  At  4/1  Wright  began  to  make  errors  and  almost  before  he 
realized  it  the  score  was  five-all.  Niles  was  all  in,  however,  and  was  not 
really   dangerous  from   then   on. 

It  was  not  until  three  days  later  that  the  rain,  which  had  started  almost 
immediately  after  the  Wright-Niles  contest,  let  up  sufficiently  to  permit  a 
continuance  of  play.  Then  it  was  decided  to  play  the  McLoughlin-Touchard 
match  on  the  championship  court.  The  ground  was  wet  and  heavy  and 
the  bound  low  and  slow.  The  balls  got  heavy  and  rackets  broke,  but  time 
was  pressing,  and  it  was  decided  to  go  on.  From  the  first  game  on  the 
positions  of  the  men  remained  unchanged.  Touchard  served  and  lost  it, 
and  McLoughlin  went  into  the  lead  to  stay.  It  was  not  a  good  match 
from  any  standpoint.  Touchard  never  once  showed  evidences  of  coming 
round,  and  McLoughlin  had  but  little  more  to  do  than  get  the  ball  back 
and  keep  doing  it.  Once  or  twice  there  was  a  rally,  which,  by  comparison, 
seemed  quite  exciting,  but  it  was  the  Westerner  who  dominated  the  situa- 
tion  from   beginning  to   end. 

Meanwhile  the  Wright-Bundy  contest  was  going  on  in  what  was  literally 
a  sea  of  mud.  The  Californian  had  objected  to  playing  under  so  unfavor- 
able conditions,  but  he  was  overruled  and  quite  unwillingly  decided  to 
make  the  best  of  it.  From  the  first  ball  he  returned  it  was  apparent  that 
the  doughty  little  warrior  was  going  to  have  his  troubles.  Tr»y  as  he 
•would,  he  could  do  nothing  with  the  heavy  spheres.  Wright,  seeing  this, 
decided  on  a  play  of  action  which  was  simply  to  get  the  ball  back  and  let 
Bundy  err.  This  he  did  to  such  good  effect  that  in  an  incredibly  short 
time  the  match  had  ended  in  his  favor  at  6/4,  6/3,  6/1.  Bundy,  who  was 
up  against  it,  realized  that  fact,  and  after  trying  vainly  and  gamely  to  get 
control  of  his  shots,  gave  it  up  as  a  bad  job.  The  day  had  afforded  any- 
thing but  satisfaction  to  players  and  spectators  alike,  but  the  worse  was  yet 
to  come,  for  the  rain  came  down  in  torrents  and  it  was  not  until  Saturday, 
three  days  later,  that  Wright  and  McLoughlin  took  the  court,  preparatory 
to    finding    which    was   to    try    his    luck    against   Champion    Larned. 

The  day  was  ideal,  but  the  same  could  not  be  said  for  the  condition  of 
the  court.  It  was  slow  and  slippery,  a  condition  supposed  to  be  to 
Wright's  advantage  and  harmful  to  the  heavy-hitting  methods  of  the 
Westerner. 

The  match  began  with  slow,  nervous  returns  by  both  men,  but  McLough- 
lin, after  a  few  tentative  wallops,  decided  that  speed  was  his  forte,  and 
thereupon  began  an  attack  which  was  too  severe  for  Wright  to  withstand. 
Only  in  the  second  set,  when  he  attempted  to  put  his  strategy  against  the 
Bostonian's,  was  there  a  time  when  his  superiority  seemed  in  danger.  It 
was  not  his  cue  to  make  the  battle  one  of  wits,  but  it  took  him  a  set  to 
find  it  out.  The  third  set  was  close,  but  the  Californian  had  the  neces- 
sary reserve  for  the  pinches.  The  fourth  set  was  one-sided,  Wright  being 
obviously  fatigued,  while  McLoughlin  played  faster  than  at  any  previous 
time. 

Although  McLoughlin  made  many  errors,  due  to  the  reckless  daring  of 
and  those  accredited  Wright.  In_  the  four  sets  he  earned  68  points  to 
his  play,  there  is  a  surprising  discrepancy  between  the  aces  he  earned 
Wright's  total  of  29.  Even  the  second  set,  which  Wright  won,  showed 
McLoughlin  to  have  earned  five  more  points  than  the  Easterner.  Of 
course  the  error  table  is  one-sided,  but  not  nearly  so  much  so  as  the 
comparison  of  earned  tallies.  An  odd  feature  of  the  match  was  that 
while  Wright  played  through  without  changing  his  racket,  McLoughlin 
broke  the  gut  of  five.  The  incident  gives  a  fair  idea  of  the  relative 
amount    of    striking    force    used    by   both    men. 

An  enormous  crowd  had  gathered  around  the  court  when  McLoughlin 
and  Larned  appeared  for  the  challenge  match.  It  was  a  perfect  day,  a 
trifle  warm,  perhaps,  but  clear  and  dry.  After  a  shout  of  welcome  had 
subsided  and  a  rather  brief  warm-up  ended,  McLoughlin  toed  the  back- 
line  and  the  fight  was  on. 


10  SPALDING'S    LAWN    TENNIS    ANNUAL. 

The  opening  game  was  close,  but  the  Californian  pulled  it  out  after  X. 
bad  start  i-love.  Larned  evened  matters  in  the  second,  for  his  opponent 
drove  into  the  net  and  out  and  failed  to  score  a  point.  The  third  and 
fourth  games  were  like  their  predecessors  and  Larned  scored  his  second 
love  game.  At  three-all  the  champion  got  three  perfect  passes  and  Ijroke 
McLoughlin's  delivery.  He  made  it  5/3,  lost  the  game,  and  took  the  set 
with  the  tenth.  So  far  Larned's  play  had  been  faultless.  He  handled 
McLoughlin's  service  easily  and  had  no  difficulty  winning  his  own.  His 
young  rival,  however,  was  having  no  end  of  trouble  with  his  ground 
strokes,  and  though  sound  on  all  other  shots,  could  not  control  his  service 
returns. 

McLoughlin  scored  four  straight  points  for  the  opening  game  of  the 
second  set,  but  his  advantage  had  a  boomerang  effect,  for  it  served  to 
stir  Larned  to  greater  efforts.  He  won  his  own  service  and  achieved  the 
remarkable  performance  of  winning  McLoughlin's  to  love.  At  4/2  Larned 
almost  succeeded  in  repeating,  but  the  youngster  staved  it  off  with  some 
great  volleying.  At  5/4  Larned  again  won  his  service  with  but  one  lost 
point,    and    the    second    set   was   over. 

The  third  saw  the  champion  quite  at  his  best.  After  allowing  the  open- 
ing game  to  get  away  from  him,  he  reeled  oft'  five  straight.  McLoughlia 
fought  hard,  but  to  no  avail.  At  1/5  he  sent  over  some  services  witk 
unbelievable  pace,  but  it  was  a  final  flash,  for,  aided  by  two  beautiful 
volleys,    Larned   took   the   seventh  and   last   game   at   love. 

Throughout  the  match  a  great  difference  was  in  evidence  between  the 
men.  Larned  was  without  a  weakness,  no  vulnerable  point  existed,  yet 
no  one  play  stood  out  superior  to  others,  so  evenly  rounded  was  his  game 
as  a  whole.  In  the  case  of  McLoughlin,  there  was  one  great  defect,  and 
that  was  a  complete  loss  of  control  at  times  on  strokes  made  off  the 
ground.  Barring  that  fatal  weakness,  his  game  was  quite  up  to  the 
standard  of   his  master  opponent.

The  Doubles  Championship 

One  of  the  most  surprising  incidents  of  the  tennis' season  occurred  when 
the  heretofore  invincible  doubles  champions,  F.  B.  Alexander  and  H  H 
Hackett,  were  defeated  by  a  new  combination,  R.  D.  Little  and  G  F* 
louchard,  in  the  challenge  round  of  the  doubles  championship. 

u-  u  °t"^'^  ^^^  ^^"^'^  holders  had  not  displayed  in  earlier  events  the  form 
which  had  won  them  the  championship,  it  was  rather  generally  supposed 
that  Newport  would  find  them  on  edge  and  fit  to  play  as  well  as  ever 
before.  Even  though  Touchard  and  Little  had  made  a  clean  sweep  of 
every  event  in  which  they  had  entered,  they  were  thought  to  be  an  inferior 
combination,  consequently  their  victory  came  in  the  nature  of  a  country- 
wide surprise. 

In  the  presence  of  an  enthusiastic  gathering  the  match  was  played  Tues- 

T^'u     'i^"^^  ^^'   ""'^^'"   conditions  that   were   ideal   in   every   respect. 

Ihe  first  set  was  excitingly  close  up  to  five-all,  at  which  point  Alexan- 
ders delivery  was  broken  through  and  Touchard  won  his  after  a  deuce 
game.  It  was  fast,  hard  tennis,  with  all  four  men  playing  at  top  speed. 
Little  was,  if  anything,  a  bit  the  best  of  the  four,  with  Hackett  a  close 
second. 

The  second  set  was  memorable  for  its  length  and  for  the  fact  that  each 
team  had  it  Within  a  point  repeatedly  before  the  champions  finally  won  it 
iNo   less   than   twenty-eight   games    were   required.      Once   Alexander   slipped 


SPALDING'S    LAWN    TENNIS    ANNUAL.  15 

as  he  was  about  to  make  the  finishing  play  and  the  opportunity  afforded 
seemed  forever  lost,  when  a  little  later  the  score  had  veered  12/13  and 
30/40  against  them.  Little,  however,  m  his  anxiety  to  end  it,  stepped  too 
far  out  of  his  own  territory,  and  he  and  Touchard  came  together  with  a 
thud,  marring  each  other's   chance  of  making  a  sure  kill. 

The  third  set  was  one-sided.  Hackett  was  obviously  tiring  and  he 
promptly  become  the  target  for  practically  all  of  the  opposition's  shots. 
Alexander's  frantic  attempts  to  come  to  his  assistance  proved  quite  futile. 
His  efforts  only  served  to  prolong  the  set  toward  its  finish,  and,  with  Little 
serving,  the  challengers  ran  it  out  at  6/2. 

The  fourth  set  was  an  odd  one.  Playing  faster,  steadier  tennis,  Hackett 
and  Alexander  forged  ahead  to  4/2,  but  a  slump  set  in  just  when  a  win 
seemed   at    hand   and    they    were    outplayed    in   the    four    succeeding   games. 

A  resume  of  the  point  score  shows  the  winners  to  have  won  208  points 
to   the   losers'    199,   a   very   slight   difference   for   a   match    so   long. 

Touchard  and  Little,  challengers,  d.  Hackett  and  Alexander,  holders, 
7/5,   ii/i3>   6/2,   6/4. 


SPALDING'S    LAWN    TENNIS    ANNUAL. 


Preliminary  Davis  Matches 

Strange  things  happen  in  the  game  of  lawn  tennis,  but  to  the  average 
American  mind  the  happening  which  had  its  beginning  at  the  West  Side 
Tennis  Club,  September  9,  was  the  strangest  that  had  occurred  in  a  long, 
long  time.  On  that  day  the  formality  of  disposing  of  Great  Britain's 
team  of  three  began,  and  ended  two  days  later,  with  the  laurels  borne  by 
America's  quartet,  to  be  sure,  but  not  before  the  men  from  across  the 
seas  had  established  their  ability  beyond  all  question  by  coming  danger- 
ously  near   to   turning  an   expected   defeat   into   an   unlooked-for   victory. 

From  every  standpoint  the  matches,  which  were  the  first  of  their  kind 
played  in  the  Metropolitan  District  since  the  time  of  the  Doherty  brothers 
invasion,  were  successful.  The  largest  crowd  that  ever  attended  a  tennis 
event  in  New  York  assembled,  and  financially  no  series  ever  approached 
the  degree  of  success  that  this  attained.  The  courts  and  management 
were  all  that  could  be  asked  for,  and  certainly  the  condition  of  the  men, 
barring  W.  A.  Larned,  could  not  have  been  improved  upon.  The  Ameri- 
can captain,  unfortunately,  was  not  feeling  fit,  but  it  did  not  prevent  his 
winning  both   of   his   matches. 

England  was  represented  by  Captain  C.  P.  Dixon,  a  veteran  of  other 
international  matches;  A.  H.  Lowe  and  A.  E.  Beamish.  America's  team 
was  composed  of  Captain  W.  A.  Larned.  M.  E.  McLoughlin,  R.  D.  Little 
and  T.  C.  Bundy.  Beamish,  Little  and  Bundy  were  played  only  in  the 
doubles,  the   latter  two  together  and  C.    P.    Dixon   with  Beamish. 

The  draw  for  the  first  day's  play  brought  together  Larned  and  Dixon. 
Lowe  was  opposed  to  McLoughlin.  The  matches  began  within  a  few  min- 
utes of  each  other.  Interest  centered  of  course,  upon  the  former  match 
and  the  court,  around  which  was  erected  the  major  portion  of  the  grand 
stand,    was   alloted   to   it. 

Ex-Champion  H.  D.  Slocum  was  chosen  as  referee  and  a  full  quota  of 
linesmen  appointed.  Dixon  won  the  toss  and  decided  to  serve.  He  got 
to  40/30,  but  Larned  broke  through  with  two  smashes  and  a  passing  fore- 
hand. The  American  played  fast  and  struck  his  stride  in  little  or  no 
time,  but  Dixon  was  not  so  quick  to  get  under  way.  At  4/0  Larned  was 
within  a  point  of  the  next  game,  but  was  defeated  in  a  pretty  driving 
duel.  At  5/1  Larned  gave  up  coming  to  the  net,  but  the  result  was  dis- 
astrous. Dixon  improved  every  moment  of  play.  Passing  his  opponent 
consistently,  he  won  two  games  and  was  40/30  for  a  third,  but  a  close 
decision   and   effective   volleying   by    Larned   ended   the   set   at   6/3. 

The  Englishman  settled  down  for  the  second  set  and  by  clever  passing 
took  Larned's  service  twice  in  succession,  besides  winning  his  own.  His 
shots  were  marvels  of  accuracy,  but  absurdly  slow.  '  Time  after  time  the 
ball  slid  by  Larned  with  so  little  momentum  that  it  looked  as  though  he 
might  easily  have  returned  it  had  he  turned  and  run  after  it.  But  the 
fastest  drives  of  McLoughlin  could  have  been  no  more  effective  than 
those  puzzling  "floaters"  which  Larned  seemed  so  utterly  unable  to  antici- 
pate with.  Dixon  leading  at  5/1,  Larned  served  and  by  better  play  took 
the  game.  On  Dixon's  service  Larned  attempted  to  follow  his  returns  to 
the   net,   but  was   passed   repeatedly. 

The  first  six  games  of  the  third  set  were  divided.  At  4/3  Larned  drove 
faultlessly,  defeating  Dixon  by  sheer,  speed  for  the  latter's  service  game. 
The  ninth,  with  the  American  serving,  was  short  and  one-sided.  Dixon's 
eye   seemed  a  trifle   off  and   he   tossed  away   several   points   on   weak   errors. 

Larned  got  off  well  for  the  fourth  set,  but  at  2/1  against  him  Dixon, 
by  wonderful  passing,  captured  three  in  a  row.  Larned  put  more  pace 
to   his   shots   in  the   seventh  gam^   and  by   placing  deep   to  the   corners   got

SPALDING'S    LAWN    TENNIS    ANNUAL.  19 

to  3/4  Again  the  Britisher  shot  the  ball  by  as  Larned  followed  in  his 
delivery,  and  the  game  score  read  5/3.  The  visitor  followed  up  his  advan- 
tage and,  as  usual,  passed  the  American  as  the   latter  came   up. 

The  fifth  set  was  a  nerve-racking  aftair  from  start  to  finish.  Larned 
opened,  but  a  deep  drive  to  one  corner  gave  Dixon  the  game.  He  took 
the  second  game,  largely  as  a  result  of  Larned's  errors,  and  divided  the 
next  four.  At  2/4  Larned  served  and  got  to  40/30,  when  Dixon  shot  a 
service  return  by  -for  deuce.  A  bad  bound  and  another  beautifully 
directed    drive    put   the   American    still    further   behind. 

Although  there  seemed  but  little  chance  for  Larned  at  that  stage,  he 
started  by  getting  a  net  cord  shot  for  the  first  point.  The  second  was  a 
■drive  past  Dixon  as  the  latter  came  in.  Larned  then  netted  two  more, 
and  the  score  was  30-all.  Dixon's  chance  seemed  at  hand,  but  he  drove 
out  and  tossed  away  the  game  with  a  double  fault.  Larned  rolled  up  his 
sleeves  then  and  served  a  fast  one  to  Dixon's  back-hand,  which  he  killed 
outright  as  it  came  back  high  and  short;  a  rally  ended  by  a  netted  shot  of 
Dixon's,  and  Larned  got  to  40/0  with  his  second  net  cord  stroke.  The 
game  ended  when  Dixon  put  one  into  the  net,  which  a  little  higher  would 
have   been   a   clean   ace. 

The  excitement  by  that  time  was  intense.  The  crowd  shouted  and 
yelled  continuously.  It  seemed  to  worry  Dixon  and  he  began  the  tenth 
game  with  a  double  fault.  Larned  made  it  15-all  by  volleying  out,  but 
hit  the  next  two  services  for  aces.  At  15/40  Dixon  was  in  a  winning 
position  to  choke  a  hard  drive,  but  for  the  third  time  in  as  many  games 
Larned's  ball  struck  the  tape  and  barely  rolled  over.  To  say  that  it  was 
exasperating  to  the  visitor  at  that  stage  of  the  game  would  be  putting  it 
mildly.      He    was   plainly    upset. 

The  next  two  games  were  short.  Dixon  shot  his  first  return  into  the 
net,  and  was  defeated  by  a  fourth  net  cord  stroke.  It  was  almost  laugh- 
able to  see  the  expression  on  the  latter's  face  as  the  ball  fell  over,  but 
no  one  could  laugh  at  such  hard  luck  and  at  such  a  time.  A  lob  which 
he  killed  outright  and  a  drive  too  fast  for  Dixon  to  handle  gave  Larned 
the   odd  game. 

The  last  game  was  one  of  the  tamest.  Each  man  made  two  error«,  but 
Larned  drove  one  out  of  reach  and  Dixon's  last  return  found  the  net. 
It  was  a  great  pull-up  for  Larned,  but  an  equally  great  disappointment  to 
Dixon.  With  the  match  all  but  lost,  Larned  cut  loose  and,  taking  big 
■chances,   ran   it   out   with   a   "Garrison    Finish." 

Dixon's  game  had  been  uniformly  excellent  and  remained  so  during 
Larned's  run.  He  played,  on  the  whole,  better  and  sounder  tennis  and 
at  no  time  was  in  danger  from  his  own  errors,  as  was  Larned.  His  style 
is  as  unusual  as  it  is  effective.  He  is  remarkably  fast  and  graceful  for 
a  man  of  his  weight  and  does  everything  with  a  minimum  of  exertion.  He 
is  so  skillful  at  conserving  his  efforts  and  eliminating  unnecessary  action 
that  he  gives  an  impression  of  being  slow,  that  is  accentuated  by  the  slow 
pace  of  his  strokes.  Dixon's  one  fault  is  the  lack  of  speed.  In  view  of 
his  exceptionally  fine  control  it  is  doubtful  if  his  inability  to  hit  hard 
may  be  termed  a  fault,  for  there  is  no  doubt  that  with  added  pace  goes 
diminishing  accuracy. 

The  McLoughlin-Lowe  match  began  with  the  former  serving  and  for 
ten  games  the  delivery  won.  As  is  so  often  the  case  with  him,  McLough- 
lin_  was  wild  and  unsteady  on  ground  strokes,  and  although  Lowe's 
delivery  was  not  a  severe  one,  the  Californian  could  not  muster  any 
•degree  of  certainly  in  his  returns.  Lowe  was  not  much  better  off,  and 
during  the  first  set  failed  dismally  to  meet  the  Westerner's'  cannon-ball 
services.  At  five-all  the  latter,  as  usual,  took  the  game  and  by  slightly 
improved   ground    play   managed   to   break   through    Lowe   for   the   set. 

In  the  second  set  McLoughlin  struck  his  stride  and  after  dropping 
Lowe's  first  service  game  ran  five  straight.  He  was  fast,  aggressive 
always,  and  never  better  overhead  and  at  volleying.     The   Englishman   was 


SPALDING'S    LAWN    TENNIS    ANNUAL.  21 

outclassed  for  the  time  and  it  began  to  look  like  a  runaway  for  the  Coast 
expert. 

With  the  third  set  a  change  began.  A  drizzle  had  made  fast  footwork 
a  precarious  thing  to  attempt,  and  much  against  his  will  McLoughlin  was 
forced  to  remain  back  most  of  the  time.  Lowe,  of  course,  came  in  only 
when  he  had  to  and  was  far  superior  to  the  American  at  driving.  The 
result  was  that  he  more  than  held  his  own.  pounding  McLoughlin's  service 
for  a  love  game  on  one  occasion  and  breaking  through  twice  afterwards. 
He  won  the   set  at  6/4,   winding  it  up   with   a   series   of   splendid   drives. 

Lowe  fell  behind  in  the  beginning  of  the  fourth  set,  but  at  1/3  against 
him  steadied  enough  to  even  up.  The  Calif ornian  made  it  4/3,  but  it  wa» 
his  last  for  the  set.  Lowe  braced  finely  and  made  it  two  sets-all  with  a 
run  of  three  games. 

The  fifth  set  began  auspiciously  for  the  Englishman.  He  took  McLough- 
lin'^ service  to  love  and  won  his  own,  but  thereafter  annexed  only  one 
more  game.  McLoughlin  played  fiercely,  hammering  everything  and  rarely 
missing.      It   was  a  great  finish   and   showed  the  youngster  at  his  best. 

The  following  Monday  the  doubles  was  played.  It  was  a  fast,  interest- 
ing match  from  beginning  to  end,  but  it  was  not  a  close  one  or  well 
played  on  the  American's  part.  Little  and  Bundy,  playing  together  for 
the  first  time,  failed  entirely  to  form  any  sort  of  team  work,  while  on  the 
other  hand  Dixon's  and  Beamish's  team  play  was  a  revelation  in  its 
harmony.  The  Englishmen  were  steady  and  clever,  but  again  the  absence 
of  any  speed  in  their  strokes,  even  smashes,  was  noticeable.  Bundy  and 
Little   were    infinitely    harder    hitters,    but    decidedly    more    erratic. 

Beamish's  play  was  watched  with  great  interest,  but  it  was  on  the 
whole  a  stereotyped  sort  of  game,  sound  enough,  but  lacking  in  origin- 
ality. All  his  strokes  were  made  correctly,  but  just  as  one  reads  of  how 
the  strokes  should  be  made.  There  was  nothing  new  in  any  play  he 
made,  nor,  on  the  other  hand,  was  there  fault  to  find  with  anything  he 
did.  Dixon  played  a  pretty  game  from  first  to  last.  He  hit  harder  than 
in   his   singles   match   and   made    even    fewer   mistakes. 

Little  was  characteristically  aggressive  and  fast.  He  used  great  speed 
on  all  his  strokes  and  his  volleying  was  the  feature  of  the  match.  In 
that  department  he  was  invincible,  catching  his  opponents  times  innumer- 
able  on   the   trickiest   sort   of   plays. 

The  weakness  of  the  team  was  due  largely  to  Bundy's  faulty  play.  The 
Californian  was  way  off  and  failed  in  all  his  efforts  to  strike  his  game. 
Ordinarily  sure  at  volleying  and  lobbing,  on  that  occasion  he  did  neither 
with  any  degree  of  accuracy.  His  lobs  were  nearly  always  short  and  his 
volleying  lacking  in  that  severity  for  which  it  is  famous.  The  match  went 
three   sets,    only   one    of   which,    the   second,    was   close. 

On  Tuesday  the  greatest  crowd  of  the  series  t-urned  out.  The  loss  of 
the  doubles  had  left  the  issue  in  doubt  and  after  the  close  matches  of 
Saturday  a  general  feeling  that  anything  might  happen  prevailed  among 
the  tennis  enthusiasts,  drawing  them  irresistibly  to  the  grounds  to  be  on 
hand   for   whatever   might    take   place. 

The  feature  was  the  Dixon-McLoughlin  set-to,  and  it  was  of  a  kind 
that  made  the  spectators  congratulate  themselves  for  having  made  the 
trip  to   the   club. 

Dixon  began  by  serving  and  won  his  game  after  deuce  was  twice  called. 
McLoughlin's  first  serve  was  a  double  fault,  but,  as  with  most  players  who 
try  to  handle  his  delivery  for  the  first  time,  Dixon  could  do  nothing  with 
it.  The  server  continued  to  win  until  five-all.  but  after  McLoughlin  broke 
through  Dixon  did  likewise.  The  youngster  repeated,  however,  and  on 
the  fourteenth  game  got  two  service  aces  and  the   first   set. 

Thus  far  there  was  but  little  to  choose.  McLoughlin  was  much  more 
severe  and  generally  aggressive,  but  Dixon  was  master  of  all  the  tricks  of 
the  game,  and  time  after  time  caught  the  Californian  off  balance  or  out- 
guessed him  in  their  maneuvers  for  position. 


R.  D.  LITTLE. 
Doubles  Champion  with  G.  F.  Touchard. 


SPALDING'S    LAWN    TENNIS    ANNUAL.  23 

McLoughlin  became  very  erratic  in  the  second  set,  throwing  away  shots 
continuously,  and  Dixon  lost  no  time  in  making  the  most  of  the  situation. 
At  4/0  McLoughlin  got  into  the  game,  succeeding  in  temporarily  stopping 
the  Englishman,  but  the  latter  was  too  steady  and  clever  to  be  permanently 
downed,   and  ran  the   set  out  at   6/3. 

The  third  set  was  as  uneven  as  the  second  had  been,  only  this  time  the 
shoe  was  on  the  other  foot.  It  was  McLoughlin  who  got  off  vi^ith  a  flying 
start  and  led  at  4/1.  Dixon  had  slacked  unconsciously  and  was  piling  up 
errors.  It  was  not  like  the  visitor  at  all,  but  his  biaces  were  short  and 
spasmodic.  At  5/3  McLoughlin  served  and  following  in  to  the  net,  volleyed 
sharply  for  the  points.  The  set  ended  with  a  drive  down  the  line  which 
Dixon,  thinking  it   would   fall   out,   let   pass. 

The  fourth  set  was  settled  quickly.  Dixon  played  as  though  very  tired. 
He  continued  to  strive  his  utmost  to  check  the  oiirush  of  his  fiery  oppo- 
nent, but  his  efforts  were  of  little  avail.  McLoughlin  got  faster  and  faster 
as  the  game  wore  on,  finishing  in  a  whirlwind  of  serving  which  his  rival 
could   not   begin   to   stop. 

Larned  and  Lowe  furnished  the  minor  attraction  of  the  afternoon,  but 
there  was  a  time  during  their  match  when  the  watchers  held  their  breath 
and  when  the  mighty  Larned  seemed  in  imminent  danger,  for  the  second- 
time  in  three  days. 

The  first  set  was  an  even  thing  for  six  games.  Neither  man  was  very 
strong  on  his  ground  strokes,  but  at  4/3  the  American  passed  Lowe  on. 
two  fast  crosscourt  shots  and  broke  through  Lowe's  service.  The  ninth 
game   was  Larned's  at  love   and   with  it   the   first   set. 

The  second  set  was  a  bad  one  for  Larned.  He  could  not  steady  and 
Lowe,  by  fine  driving  and  an  occasional  volley  ace,  took  five  straight 
games.  The  next  was  Larned's,  but  the  seventh  ended  the  set,  for  Lowe's 
delivery   was   driven   out  and  into   the   net  twice,   each   way. 

During  the  set  Larned  played  nervously  and  seemed  to  be  constantly 
trying  to  find  his  swing.  He  failed  utterly  to  get  going  and  was  no  better 
at  the  beginning  of  the   set  than   he   was  at  its  end. 

Lowe  kept  on  with  his  winning  streak  until  the  score  stood  3/1  in  the 
third  set.  He  had  been  going  at  a  fast  clip  and  instinctively  slowed  dowr 
at  that  point  to  ease  the  strain.  That  it  was  fatal  was  proven  instantly. 
for  Larned  raced  ahead,  putting  twice  the  pace  to  his  shots  and  never  for 
a  second  letting  up.  At  that  Lowe  managed  to  win  two  more  games,  but 
at  five-all   Larned   pounded   out  two   games  and   his   second   set. 

The  fourth  set  was  over  in  less  time  than  it  takes  to  tell  it.  Larned 
played  faster,  more  accurately,  and  took  the  net  oftener.  At  5/0  Lowe 
won  a  game,  but  the  next  one   ended  the  match. 

Lowe's  game  was  favorably  commented  upon  by  many  experts  who  were 
near  the  side  lines,  but  it  was  not  in  any  sense  a  well  rounded  one.  Off 
the  ground  his  play  was  excellcTit  and  his  peculiar  delivery  seemed  quite 
effective.  His  volleying  was  not  to  be  compared  with  his  other  strokes,  nor 
was  his  smashing  severe  enough  to  be  at  all   effective. 

The  summaries:

Britain),  6/3,  2/6,  6/3,  3/6.  7/5;  M.  E.  McLoughlin  (America)  d.  A.  H. 
Lowe    (Great   Britain),  8/6,   6/1,   4/6,  4/6,   6/3. 

Monday,  September  11. — C.  P.  Dixon  and  A.  E.  Beamish  (Great  Britain) 
d.   R.   D.   Little  and  T.  C.   Bundy    (America).  6/3,    7/5,  6/4.

(Great  Britain),  8/6,  3/6,  6/3,  6/2;  W.  A.  Larned  (America)  d.  A.  H. 
Lowe    (Great  Britain),  6/3,    1/6,   7/5,  6/1.

SPALDING'S    LAWN     TENNIS    ANNUAL. 


Davis  Cup  Final 

Although  universally  conceded  to  have  sent  the  strongest  team  which 
could  be  mustered,  America's  third  attempt  to  wrest  from  Australasia  the 
world-famous  Davis  Cup  proved  as  futile  as  its  predecessors,  and,  in  spite 
of  the  addition  of  Captain  W.  A.  Larned,  the  visitors  went  down  to  a 
defeat  as   disappointing  as  it  was  decisive. 

N.  E.  Brookes,  A.  W.  Dunlop  and  Rod  Heath  were  the  men  who  suc- 
cessfully withstood  the  assault  of  the  challengers.  The  vain  efforts  for  the 
United  States  were  made  by  W.  A.  Larned,  B.  C.  Wright  and  M.  E. 
McLoughlin.  The  latter  trio,  champion,  ex-champion  and  most  promising 
youngster  ever  produced  in  this  or  any  other  country,  made  a  sorry  show- 
ing for  men  of  their  recognized  ability.  Not  one  of  the  five  points  possible 
to  earn  were  credited  to  them,  and  but  one  of  the  four  matches  actually 
played  went  the  limit  of  five  sets. 

This,  in  the  face  of  the  fact  that  Australasia's  team  was  by  no  means 
considered  the  strongest  that  might  have  been  put  in  the  field,  undoubtedly 
created  a  world-wide  surprise  and  astonishment  in  tennis  circles.  So  gen- 
eraliy  was  the  feeling  of  America's  superiority  entertained  that  up  to  the 
very  day  of  the  first  matches  played  speculation  favored  them,  and  what 
betting  was  done  showed  the  cup  defenders  to  be  on  the  short  end,  even 
in  the  judgment  of  their  most  ardent   supporters. 

November  i  saw  the  Americans  leave  Vancouver  aboard  the  steamship 
Zelandia  and,  according  to  schedule,  a  month  or  more  intervened  between 
the  time  of  their  arrival  in  Christchurch,  New  Zealand,  and  the  dates  set 
for  the  matches,  December  29,  30  and  January  i.  That  time  was  utilized 
for  practice,  and  it  was  not  long  before  sea  legs  disappeared  and  daily 
improvementlwas  apparent  in  each.  Wright  was  the  first  to  come  around, 
with  Larned  improving,  and  McLoughlin  a  trifie  slow  to  attain  a  normal 
stride.  Day  after  day  they  tried  each  other  out,  but,  from  indications 
which  were  subsequently  borne  out,  Wright  and  Larned  had  the  call  for 
tingles,  with  McLoughlin  and  the  Bostonian,  the  previously  agreed  upon 
team  in  doubles. 

Improving  steadily,  the  Americans  approached  the  day  set  for  the  battle 
to  begin,  when  the  most  disastrous  of  occurrences  came  about.  Larned  began 
to  show  the  effects  of  continued  damp  and  inclement  weather  in  the  form 
of  rheumatism  in  the  left  ankle  and  right  elbow.  In  spite  of  treatment 
and  every  precaution  his  condition  failed  to  improve,  and  on  the  day 
previous  to  the  first  matches  the  American  champion  intimated  his  attention 
of  withdrawing  to  F.  M.  B.  Fisher,  the  referee.  At  the  last  moment,  how- 
ever, he  decided  against  defaulting  or  the  substitution  of  McLoughlin  and 
was  drawn  against  Heath  in  the  second  match  of  the  opening  day.  Wright 
drew  Brookes,  and  to  these  old  rivals  fell  the  honor  of  commencing 
hostilities. 

Steady  rain  made  it  impossible  to  play  on  Friday,  December  28,  and  it 
was  not  until  Monday  that  conditions  warranted  a  beginning.  By  one- 
thirty,  the  time  set  for  the  opening  match,  every  seat  was  occupied,  and  a 
huge,  enthusiastic  Crowd  greeted  Brookes  and  Wright,  who  appeared 
punctually  on  time. 

The  American  won  the  toss  and  elected  to  serve.  A  brief  warm-up, 
followed  by  a  hush  from  the  crowd,  and  the  fight  between  the  two  world- 
famotis  left-handers  began. 

Wright's  first  service  was  driven  out  of  court,  each  netted,  and  then  a 
well-placed  service,  followed  by  a  winning  cross-court  volley,  and  the 
American  had  drawn  first  blood.  Wright  got  to  30-love  on  Brookes'  deliv 
ery  in  the  second  game,  but  that  snake-like  service  soon  got  its  work  in 
and  the  game  score   was  quickly  tied.     The  third  game   was  a  bad  one  for 


E.    McLUUGHLlN    AND    B.    C.    WKIGHT    DLKING    THE 
BROOKES    AND    DUNLOP.

DUNLOP    AND    BROOKKS    IN    THE    DOUBLES.

SPALDING'S    LAWN     TENNIS    ANNUAL.  27 

the  visitor.  From  30-love  he  volleyed  v^^eakly  double-faulted,  and  then 
becoming  momentarily  confused,  dropped  two  more  points.  He  made  two 
beautiful  shots  in  the  fourth  game,  but  they  were  not  enough,  and  the 
score  went  up  to  3/1.  Though  Brookes  made  uniformly  excellent  returns 
in  the  next,  Wright  was  keen  and  on  his  toes  every  second,  anne>{ing  the 
game  on  wonderful  volleying.  Three  times  in  the  sixth  Wright  found  the 
net  on  his  opening  drives  and  ended  the  agony  with  a  poor  volley  into  the 
bottom  of  the  barrier.  At  3/4  Brookes'  accuracy  went  askew  and  he  barely 
missed  the  side  lines  twice.  He  was  aided  then  by  a  double  and  a  win- 
ning block  of  a  fast  chop.  A  wretched  smash  of  a  weak  lob  and  a  cross- 
court  chop  that  was  just  out  lost  the  game  for  the  American.  The  latter 
then  broke  through  Brookes  for  the  ftrst  time  one  point  being  a  net  cord 
play,  but  the  Australasian  crowded  on  steam  in  the  tenth  and  his  opponent 
was  out  of  it  entirely   for  the  time. 

It  was  a  fast,  exciting  set,  with  Wright  the  aggressor  but  less  steady,, 
and  Brookes  cold,  calculating  and,  as  usual,  taking  full  advantage  of  the 
smallest  opportunities. 

Although  endeavoring  in  every  conceivable  way  to  follow  the  advantage 
thus  gained,  Brookes  was  fairly  smothered  by  the  tremendously  daring  yet 
accurate  play  of  his  opponent  in  the  second  set.  No  matter  where  he  put 
the  ball  Wright  tried  for  it,  with  the  result  that  numberless  seemingly 
point-winning  plays  were  picked  up  and  turned  against  hirn.  The  Ameri- 
can, by  his  strenuous  labors,  allowed  his  rival  but  four  points  in  the  first 
three  games  and  almost  captured  the  fourth  at  1/3.  Brookes  got  to  40/15^ 
but  put  two  in  succession  over  the  base  line,  allowing  Wright  to  make  it, 
4/1.  Meantime,  Wright  was  exhausting  an  enormous  amount  of  energy 
and  strength  in  his  body-racking  starting  and  stopping  and  twisting  and 
turning.  It  seemed  that  a  reaction  must  come  at  any  second  and  with  it 
Brookes'  chance.  At  5/1  Wright  dropped  his  second  game,  and  then,  with 
Brookes  serving,  a  fiercely  contested,  stubborn  fight  began.  No  less  than 
twenty-two  points  were  required  to  decide  the  game  and  each  apparently 
had  it  won  a  dozen  times.  Once  Wright  made  a  miraculous  save  in  the 
form  of  a  pick-up  and  followed  it  with  a  volley  that  whistled  through 
Brookes  for  the  point  and   set. 

Wild  applause  greeted  the  visitor's  success,  for  indications  pointed'  ta 
another  1908  struggle,  and  it  is  for  such  contests  that  spectators  travel  far 
to  see  and  to  which  they  give  a  permanent  nook  in  their  memories. 

However,  the  physical  effort  which  Brookes  had  made  up  to  that  point 
was  as  nothing  compared  to  the  exhausting  tax  Wright  had  been  forced  to, 
and  the  third  set  found  the  Australian  comparatively  fresh  and  quite  at 
his  best. 

Wright  opened  and  lost  one  point  in  the  game,  but  Brookes  retaliated 
with  a  love  game  in  the  second.  Wright  returned  the  compliment  in  kind 
and  a  minute  later  Brookes  made  it  two-all.  It  was  then  that  Wright 
showed  the  effects  of  his  efforts.  He  was  forced  to  let  up  and  Brookes 
jumped  away,  winning  three  successive  games.  Wright  came  back  and, 
with  clever  passing,  took  Brookes'  service,  but  it  was  a  momentary  flash, 
for  Brookes  did  likewise  to  the  American's  delivery,  and  the  all-important 
third  set  ended  at  6/3.  „r  •  . 

The  third  set  was  merely  a  forerunner  of  what  was  to  follow.  Wright 
was  done.  Tired,  he  continued  to  fight,  making  many  fine  shots,  but 
Brookes,  a  Napoleon  for  strategy  and  a  miniature  giant  of  strength,  was  a. 
veritable  stone  wall  into  which,  no  matter  which  way  he  turned,  the  Ameri- 
can ran.  Brookes'  volleying  was  perfect,  flawless,  and  as  he  camped  at 
the  net  and  would  not  be  dislodged  with  Wright's  best  plays,  the  result 
was  never  in  doubt.  It  was  a  set  intensely  interesting,  not  from  the  stand- 
point of  a  contest,  but  for  what  Brookes,  with  his  genius  for  the  game, 
would  do  next.  The  spectators  were,  like  Wright,  utterly  unable  to  fathom 
or  possess  the  Australian's  next  move,  and  therein  lay  the  secret  of  the 
man's  success. 


SPALDING'S    LAWN     TENNIS    ANNUAL.  29 

After  a  brief  intermission,  in  which  time  was  taken  to  roll  down  the 
court,  Larned  and  Heath  appeared.  The  American  got  off  well,  winning 
Heath's  service  and  his  own.  Both,  during  the  beginning,  remained  back, 
driving  rather  weakly  and  apparently  intent  upon  safety.  Heath  retaliated 
by  placing  the  third  and  fourth  games  to  his  credit,  but  it  was  his  total 
for  the  set.  Larned  played  fast  and  accurately  and  ran  out  four  games  in 
good  shape. 

It  was  then  that  the  unaccountable  happened.  Heath  got  to  2-love  before 
Larned  tallied.  He  was  playing  soundly  now,  brilliantly  occasionally,  but 
always  doing  a  bit  more  than  had  been  expected  from  him,  yet  his  game 
was  certainly  not  of  that  class  or  calibre  to  hold,  much  less  defeat,  the 
great  Larned.  Something  was  wrong,  and  Larned's  game  for  the  time  was 
pitiable  in  its  helplessness.  Heath  got  the  set  after  the  loss  of  that  third 
game,  and  then  began  the  struggle  for  the  odd  one.  Again  Larned  jumped 
away,  and  again  something  happened  to  break  up  his  game.  From  a  win- 
ning position  at  4/2  the  visitor  suddenly  broke.  Shots  usually  good  for 
the  point  went  wild,  and  it  was  the  backstop  and  net  which  the  majority 
of  his  returns  found.  There  was  no  apparent  reason  for  it.  It  was  not 
due  to  a  great  improvement  on  Heath's  part.  It  was  altogether  unaccount- 
able, unreasonable,  yet  it  happened,  and  Heath  forged  steadily  on,  rolling 
up  points.     The   fourth  set  was  a  walkover. 

Larned,  lame  and  utterly  helpless,  without  poise  or  control,  was  defeated. 
Defeated  by  a  combination  of  his  own  errors  and  his  opponent's  sound, 
steady  play.  It  was  a  surprise,  and  disappointment  shared  by  the  multi- 
tude, who  for  the  first  time  saw  the  world-famous  veteran  in  action,  that 
has  rarely  if  ever  been  equalled  in  the  history  of  the  game.  That  Larned 
"was  not  himself,  was  ill  and  mentally  unfit,  was  perfectly  apparent.  That 
he  was  able  to  play  at  all  was  merely  an  evidence  of  his  grit  and  deter- 
mination. A  physician's  certificate  to  the  elYect  that  he  was  unfit  to  play 
had  little  effect.  So  far  as  his  decision  to  take  part  was  concerned  and 
■because  such  were  the  circumstances,  it  is  difficult  to  draw  conclusions  or 
to  give  to  Heath  the  full  measure  of  credit,  which,  under  other  conditions, 
■would  be  rightfully  his.  The  winner  played  well,  better  than  it  was  sup- 
posed he  could,  but  for  all  that  there  will  always  remain  the  doubt,  the 
possibility  or  dissention  which  a  victory  so  lacking  in  decisiveness  must 
necessarily  entail. 

However  dark  the  prospects  at  this  point  in  the  series  may  have  looked 
to  the  Americans,  there  was  no  evidence  of  discouragement  shown.  That 
Larned  would  yet  come  round  was  the  hope  they  bore,  consequently  it  was 
•with  a  grim  determination  that  Wright  and  McLoughlin  took  the  court  on 
the  second  day  of  the  series.  It  was  McLoughlin's  first  appearance  as  well 
as  A.  W.  Dunlop's  entry  into  the  fight,  and  a  crowd  of  over  four  thousand 
were  on  hand  to  watch  the  turning  point  or  end,  as  the  fortunes  of  war 
might  decree. 

Wright  won  the  toss  and  chose  his  own  service  with  which  to  begin. 
The  game  was  short,  marred  by  a  double  fault  at  a  critical  point,  and  was 
won  by  the  Australasians.  Brookes  followed  in  the  order  of  delivery  and 
3-love  was  quickly  called.  Then  it  was  McLoughlin's  turn,  and  the 
*'Comet,"  as  he  was  dubbed  by  his  enthusiastic  admirers,  promptly  set 
things  aflame.  Four  sizzling  services  and  a  volley  that  streaked  through 
Dunlop  for  an  ace  wound  up  a  love  game.  The  crowd  roared  its  approval 
*nd  continued  to  applaud  as  McLoughlin  and  Wright  broke  Dunlop's 
service  for  two-all.  But  again  Wright  fai*«??l  to  win  his  service.  He  was 
too  careful  and  endeavored  to  play  too  often  to  Dunlop,  with  the  result 
that  his  volleying  was  very  ineffective.  Too  late  in  the  set  he  awoke  to 
his  errors  of  judgment,  and  although  he  won  his  third  service  game  to 
love,  the  brace  came  at  a  time  when  the  damage  had  been  done,  and  the 
Pet   went  to   the   cup  defenders   at   6/4. 

The  second  set  began  with  the  "California  Comet's"  service  and  he  shut 
out  his  opponents,  four  points  to  love.     Brookes  and  Dunlop  each  suffered 


SPALDING'S    LAWN     TENNIS    ANNUAL,  31 

the  indignity  of  being  unable  to  even  get  their  rackets  on  the  ball. 
Throughout  the  set  the  youngster  played  in  whirlwind  fashion,  killing  and 
volleying  with  absolutely  unapproachable  speed  and  certainty.  Never  were 
lobs  treated  with  such  scant  consideration  or  services  shot  back  as  though 
from  the  mouth  of  a  cannon.  It  was  a  pace  too  fast  for  Brookes  or  Dun- 
lop  to  cope  with,  and  the  set  went  to  the  Americans'   credit  in  short  order. 

Again  it  fell  to  McLoughlin  to  begin  the  set,  and  for  the  second  time  a 
love  game  was  tallied  at  the  beginning.  As  the  fight  had  become  largely  a 
matter  of  services,  it  was  a  question  of  where  the  break  was  coming.  Not 
from  Brookes  or  McLoughlin,  for  each  was  delivering  with  heart-breaking 
regularity  almost  unplayable  balls.  It  therefore  was  up  to  Dunlop  and 
Wright,  and  finally,  at  five-all,  Wright  fell  down.  Not  literally,  but  figur- 
atively; he  lost  his  punch.  _  Only  one  point  was  credited  him  and  with 
Brookes  following,  the  Americans'  chance  for  the  set  went  glimmering.  Up 
to  that  point  in  the  match  it-  was  Wright  who  trailed  the  held  and  whose 
play  on  the  whole  was  below  par.  His  service  was  the  least  effective,  and 
his  whole  game  below  that  standard  of  which  he  is  capable.  Brookes'  form 
was  characteristic  of  that  great  player,  and  that  being  so,  no  further  com- 
ment is  necessary.  His  partner,  Dunlop,  was  on  edge,  and  as  he  is  one 
of  the  really  great  doubles  men,  his  value  may  well  be  imagined.  Over- 
head and  at  volleying  he  was  irresistible.  Only  his  service  may  be  said  to 
have  been  weak,  but  as  both  he  and  Brookes  covered  it  with  phenomenal 
volleying,  it  was  a  point  which,  though  vulnerable,  did  not  stand  out  as 
did  Wright's.  McLoughlin  was,  as  'tis  said,  "all  there."  Nothing  troubled 
him  during  that  stage  of  the  game,  unless  it  was  an  occasional  tendency 
to  "pull"  his  smashes,  due  to  the  insistent  demand  from  Wright  for  caution. 

But  the  fourth  set  did  not  go  the  way  of  its  predecessors.  Here,  at  last, 
the  fight  became  more  one-sided.  True,  the  challengers  got  off  to  a  flying 
start,  but  it  was  not  long  before  they  were  levelled.  At  2-love  it  was 
'  Wright's  service,  but  he  tailed  to  improve.  Obviously  it  was  not  his  day. 
Everything  he  did  was  just  a  trifle  off.  He  was  not  playing  badly.  It 
seemed  more  like  hard  luck.  His  shots  just  missed,  but  that  little  was  as 
disastrous  as  if  he   was  dropping  them  yards  beyond   the   boundaries. 

Losing  that  game  was  the  beginning  of  a  slump  which  of  itself  settled 
the  possession  of  the  cup  for  another  year.  Brookes  and  Dunlop  took  four 
straight,  winning  from  McLoughlin  for  the  first  time.  Here  Wright  braced 
and  won  his  service  and  followed  by  breaking  through  Brookes  for  four- 
all.  Then,  with  McLoughlin  up  and  Dunlop  to  follow,  the  chances  looked 
bright,  but  instantly  faded  when  the  Californian,  for  the  second  time, 
weakened  and  allowed  his  opponents  to  get  the  advantage  again.  The  tenth 
spelled  "finis"  for  the  Americans.  Not  a  point  was  won  by  them  and  the 
series  was  over,  so  far  as  the  result  as  a  whole   was  concerned. 

Meantime,  Larned  had  failed  to  improve,  and  it  had  been  decided  to 
play  McLoughlin  in  his  place  against  Brookes.  Great  disappointment  was 
felt  when  it  became  known  that  Brookes  and  Larned  were  not  to  meet.  A 
match  between  them  was  what  the  tennis  world  had  been  looking  forward 
to  ever  since  the  Australian  captured  the  English  title  in  1908,  and  when 
at  the  eleventh  hour  the  meeting  was  declared  oft  and  the  question  of 
supremacy  between  these  two,  most  famous  and  of  greatest  ability,  in  all 
the  world,  it  took  from  the  series  an  interest  which  no  other  match  could 
supply. 

However,  no  one  was  prepared  for  the  surprise  of  the  third  day.  Wright 
scratched  to  Heath,  and  the  only  match  was  that  between  Brookes  and 
McLoughlin.  With  a  memory  of  what  had  happened  to  the  youngster  in 
his  previous  meeting  with  Brookes,  the  spectators  expected  but  little  from 
him.  Therefore,  his  fight  so  nearly  successful,  was  a  revelation  to  the 
hundreds  who  had  seen  him,  a  helpless  novice  in  the  power  of  his  crafty 
opponent   but  two   years  ago. 

Perfect  weather  conditions,  a  hard  court  and  huge  crowd  greeted  the 
combatants  as  they   stepped  on  the   court.     There   was  nothing  unusual  for 


SPALDING'S    LAWN     TENNIS     ANNUAL.  33 

the  first  six  games.  Each  won  three  on  services,  with  McLoughlin  having 
the  advantage  of  delivery  on  the  odd  game.  The  seventh  game  was  close. 
Brookes  volleyed  an  easy  drive  too  deep  when  the  score  stood  30/40,  but 
after  deuce  was  called  passed  and  lobbed  over  his  rival  for  the  necessary 
aces.  He  won  his  own  service  for  5/3,  but  could  do  nothing  against  the 
tremendous  speed  which  the  *'Comet"  imparted  to  his  delivery  in  the  next. 
In  the  tenth  game  McLoughlin  hit  the  net.  drove  out,  fumbled  a  low  one 
at  his  feet,  drove  another  into  the  barrier,  and  found  himself  one  set  down. 
Nothing  daunted,  he  set  sail  in  the  second  set  and  soon  had  Brookes  in 
deep  water.  After  losing  the  first  game  he  won  three  straight,  with  13 
aces  to  6.  He  was  hitting  with  tremendous  power,  recklessly,  and  yet 
without  a  trace  of  wildness.  Brookes  was  temporarily  outclassed,  being 
fairly  smothered  by  speed.  The  fifth  game  started  auspiciously  for 
McLoughlin,  for  his  first  three  services  were  unreturned.  He  double- 
faulted  next  and  was  outvolleyed  twice.  He  got  the  vantage  point  on  a 
volley,  and  then  missing  his  first  service,  sent  his  second  with  such  pace 
and  direction  that  Brookes  failed  to  touch  it,  4/1.  Brookes,  volleying  in 
the  next  game,  checked  the  Californian's  stampede,  but  in  the  seventh 
game  Brookes  was  defeated,  four  points  to  love,  and  each  point  a  perfect 
volley.  At  5/2  Brookes  won  his  service,  but  McLoughlin  sent  over  the 
delivery  in  the  eighth  game  with  such  fierce  speed  that  set-all  was  called 
in  jig  time. 

The  crowd  cheered  and  then  settled  back  with  the  evident  belief  that 
here  was  a  match  that  would  not  soon  be  over,  and  an  opponent  for 
Brookes  that  was  going  to  keep  that  gentleman  rather  busy.  The  third  set 
opened  with  Brookes  serving.  He  got  to  30-all  before  McLoughlin  tried  a 
tentative  bang,  which  flew  past  like  a  cannon  shot.  Evidently  satisfied 
that  his  artillery  was  still  in  working  order,  he  tried  two  more  with  the 
same  result.  Brookes  deuced  it  with  a  clever  back-hander,  but  the  young- 
ster  swatted  two  beauties  and   captured  the  game. 

Brookes'  expression  remained  unchanged  as  his  opponent  thus  assumed 
a  lead  and  imperturable  as  ever  he  grappeled  with  the  American's  service 
to  such  good  effect  that  i-all  was  recorded.  A  streak  of  wild  hitting  let 
Brookes  in  for  the  third  game  rather  easily,  but  McLoughlin  soon  steadied 
and  with  the  aid  of  a  lucky  volley  made  it  2-all.  Brookes  took  his  own 
service  without  serious  opposition  in  the  fifth  game,  but  another  streak  of 
phenomenally  brilliant  play  by  the  "Comet"  left  him  far  behind,  with  a 
total  of  five  points  scored  to  his  opponents'  twelve.  At  5/3  the  Australian 
crept  up  a  game  closer,  but  again  McLoughlin,  serving,  was  irresistible, 
and  a  love  game  found  him  leading  at  two  sets  to   one. 

The  crowd  roared  its  approval.  Partisanship  was  a  thing  unknown,  for- 
gotten, and  the  greeting  accorded  the  red-haired  youth  was  an  unbiased 
tribute  to  his  natural  brilliancy  and  spectacularly  reckless  style  of  play. 
Brookes  scored  the  first  game  of  the  fourth  set  after  deuce  was  called,  and 
a  little  later  ran  up  to  3/1;  3/1.  3/2,  4/2,  4/3  and  5/3  was  the  order  of 
games  thereafter.  McLoughlin  had  slowed  down  a  bit  and  Brookes  was 
handling  the  situation  with  more  of  his  accustomed  poise  and  ease.  Sud- 
denly crowding  on  pace  he  caught  McLoughlin  unprepared  and  ran  out  on 
his  own  service,  making  the  score  two  sets  all. 

A  hushed  excitement  fell  over  the  crowd  when  the  fifth  set  began.  The 
cup  was  won  and  there  was  consequently  nothing  to  fear  from  the  result 
of  this  match.  A  win  by  the  youngster  would  be  as  warmly  received  as 
a  reverse  result,  and  the  lack  of  anxiety  enhanced  the  joy  of  watching  the 
two  master  hands  fight  to  a  finish. 

Again  McLoughlin  had  the  advantage  of  opening  on  service.  Relent 
lessly  determined  as  Brookes  was  now,  he  was  forced  to  bow  to  that  unbeat- 
able delivery,  and  though  he  forced  the  score  to  deuce  he  was  unable  to 
do  better.  He  won  the  second  game  to  15  by  inimitable  volleying  and 
which   forced    McLoughlin    to    err.      The   third   game    went   to    the   latter   at 


F.  G.   LOWE. 


A.    E.    BEAMISH. 


SPALDING'S    LAWN    TENNIS    ANNUAL.  35 

love.  Two  winning  volleys,  a  fast  dropping  drive  and  service  act, 
acccounted   for  it. 

The  fourth  game  was  a  battle  royal.  At  15-all  Brookes  slipped  and  fell, 
but  was  uninjured.  Finally,  after  twenty  points  had  been  won  and  lost. 
Brookes  got  one  of  his  famous  backhand  volleys  across  court  and  the 
score  was  tied  at  2-all.  Every  variety  of  play  had  been  put  into  use  in 
that  game,  and  at  the  end  there  was  but  little  to  choose  in  the  manner 
each   man  had   made   his  strokes. 

Brookes  had  a  great  chance  for  the  fifth  game.  After  paving  the  way 
to  15/40  he  was  outvolleyed  twice,  and  McLoughlin  ran  it  out  with  a 
thundering  smash  and  service  ace.  The  sixth  game  was  another  hotly  con- 
tested session,  but  Brookes,  volleying,  was  too  good,  and  he  evened  the 
game  score. 

Then  the  unexpected  happened.  McLoughlin  dropped  his  service. 
Brookes  passed  him  twice,  and  a  net  and  out  by  the  server  ended  the 
game.  At  top  speed  the  Australian  captain  followed  up  his  advantage,  and 
with  the  loss  of  but  one  set  made  it  5/3.  McLoughlin  tightened  up  in  the 
ninth  and  help  a  safe  lead  till  5/4  was  announced.  A  deuce  set  looked 
imminent  when  two  side  line  passes  left  Brookes  to  gaze  at  where  the  ball 
went  by,  but  that  was  the  youngster's  final  stand.  Four  successive  aces  by 
Brookes   on   volleys   ended   the   match. 

For  another  year  at  least  the  Davis  Cup  remains  unchanged.  No  more 
valiant  or  successful  defense  of  the  famous  trophy  could  be  made  than 
that  of  the  allied  forces  of  Australia  and  New  Zealand. 

SUMMARIES. 

Monday,  January  i. — First  match:  N.  E.  Brookes  (Australasia)  d.  B. 
C.  Wright  (America),  6/3,  2/6,  6/3,  6/3.  Second  match:  R.  W.  Heath 
(Australasia)   d.  W.  A.   Larned   (America).  2/6,  6/1,   7/5,   6/2.
