SPALDING'S    LAWN    TENNLS     ANNUAL.  37 

The  Meeting   of   the  Sectional^  Doubles 
Winners  at  Chicago 

The  day  afted  Bull  and  Martin  had  annexed  the  Western  Championship, 
Tuesday,  August  i,  the  draw  for  the  preliminary  doubles,  played  at  the 
Onwentsia  Club  of  Lake  Forest,  was  made,  and  as  luck  would  have  it,  the 
two  teams  conceded  to  be  the  strongest  were  compelled  to  meet.  They 
were  McLoughlin  and  Bundy,  winners  of  the  Pacific  Coast  elimination 
series,  and  Touchard  and  Little,  who  had  won  the  Longwood  event,  the 
classic   of  the   East. 

That  left  Bull  and  Martin  to  face  the  South's  representatives,  C.  B. 
and  H.  L.  Doyle.  The  first  match  played  was  the  former.  It  was  con- 
tested on  a  court  a  trifle  slow  from  a  rainy  morning,  before  the  largest 
crowd  ever  gathered  in  the  West  to  watch  a  tennis  match.  Touchard  was 
the  first  to  serve  and  had  little  difficulty  in  winning  the  game  to  fifteen. 
Bundy  followed,  but  only  managed  to  win  one  point;  Little,  however, 
was  defeated  at  love,  and  with  McLoughlin  to  follow  it  looked  like  two-all. 
A  long  game  resulted,  which  ended  finally  when  Little  drove  one  too  hard 
for  Bundy  to  handle.  The  next  game  was  the  Easterners,  but  Bundy 
made  it  4/2  a  moment  later.  Little  took  his  service  to  love,  but  the  lead 
of  5/2  thus  gained  appeared  done  for  when  the  Westerners  took  two 
straight  with  only  three  points  lost.  Bundy,  however,  was  hammered  hard 
in  the  tenth  game,  and  the  set  ended  when  Touchard  passed  McLoughlin 
down   his   alley. 

The  California  pair  got  away  to  a  better  start  in  the  second  set,  but 
from  a  two-love  lead  they  were  overhauled,  eventually  losing  the  set  by 
a  score  of  6/4.  Throughout  the  two  sets  Touchard  and  McLoughlin  were 
in  far  better  form  than  their  partners.  The  former  was  at  his  best,  but 
McLoughlin  was  at  times  phenomenally  brilliant  and  at  others  in  his  most 
erratic  mood.  Bundy  was  the  weakest  of  th^  quartet,  lobbing  short  and 
lacking  pace  on  all  his  returns.  Little  was  very  wild  at  first,  but  improved 
steadily,  and  at  the  close  of  the  second  set  had  approached  form  charac- 
teristic  of  him   at  his  best. 

The  third  set  was  a  fight  from  start  to  finish.  Bundy  was  made  the 
object  of  attack  by  the  New  York  pair,  but  instead  of  crumpling  before 
it  he  met  it  at  every  turn  and  showed  a  general  improvement  remarkable 
for  its  aggressive  strength.  After  an  alternation  of  games  to  seven-all 
Little's  service  was  won  by  the  Californians,  and  the  next,  with  McLoughlin 
serving,   ended   the   set. 

The  short  rest  that  followed  worked  wonders  in  McLoughlin  and  Bundy 
and  after  an  even  break  to  three-all  they  went  ahead  to  stay,  winning  at 
6/3.  Touchard  had  gone  off  in  that  set,  and  though  Little  was  going 
splendidly,  his  partner's  slump  proved  too  big  a  handicap  for  him  to  beat. 

The  fifth  set  was  watched  with  intense  interest.  By  all-round  superior 
play  the  Californians  managed  to  gain  a  lead  of  4/2,  but  Little  served  and 
took  the  game  for  4/3.  Bundy  followed,  and  aided  by  two  great  smashes 
of  McLoughlin's,  gave  his  side  another  game  lead.  A  long  struggle  for 
Touchard's  service  followed,  but  after  deuce  was  called  three  times,  it 
finally  went  to  his  credit.  It  looked  to  be  all  over  then,  for  McLoughlin's 
delivery  had  been   invincible   for  two   sets. 

The  first  point  was  an  ace  straight  down  the  middle  by  Touchard,  and 
Little  made  it  0/30  by  driving  cross  court  as  McLoughlin  rushed  in.  The 
game  ended  when,  at  30/40,  McLoughlin  smashed  a  deep  lob  some  ten  feet 
over  the  base  line.  Again  at  six-all  the  Westerners  took  the  lead  by  driv- 
ing   Touchard's    service    for    repeated    aces,    and    again    McLoughlin,    after 


38  SPALDING'S   LAWN    TENNIS    ANNUAL. 

getting  to  40/30,  failed  to  put  over  the  winning  stroke.  Little  made  a 
miraculous  save  of  a  hard-dropping  drive  and  the  game  score  was  a 
moment  later  evened. 

At  last,  when  8-all  was  called.  McLoughlin  had  his  third  chance  but 
his  attempt  to  get  by  was  woefully  weak.  The  match  ended  when  Littl- 
served  out  his  gan^e  to  30.  The  last  point  was  a  drive,  which,  had  it  gone 
by  would  undoubtedly  have  dropped  out,  but  it  was  so  fast  that  Bundy 
could  not  get  his  racket  out  of  the  way  in  time,  and  the  ball  caught  it  and 
deflected   high  up  into   the   backstop. 

Immediately  after  the  match  ended  Bull  and  Martin  took  the  court  with 
the  Doyles.  It  was  early  apparent  that  Bull  was  still  suffering  from  the 
gruelling  effect  of  his  match  the  day  before  against  Church  and  Mathey. 
He  could  not  get  under  way  and  seemed  slack  and  lifeless.  Martin  too 
was  in  far  from  his  best  form,  but  the  Doyles  were  on  edge  and  quick  to' 
strike  their  gate.  The  latter  pair  lacked  speed,  but  that  defect  was  offset 
by  their  accurate  placing  and  bewildering  formations.  The  tactics  of  the 
brothers  were  as  unusual  as  they  were  effective.  Original  positions  they 
certainly  were  and  in  absolute  opposition  to  the  recognized  formations  of 
that  mysterious  enigma   termed    "good   form." 

The  first  set  was  won  by  the  Washingtonians  at  6/3  and  the  second  at 
8/6.  In  the  latter  the  tennis  was  faster  and  all  four  men  played  better 
but  Bulls  service  went  against  him  in  the  thirteenth  game  and  the  set 
ended   with   H.   E.    ("Rol")    Doyle   serving. 

The  third  set  was  a  walkover  for  the  brothers.  In  the  first  four  games 
the  New  \  ork  team  won  only  six  points.  The  fifth  was  theirs,  but  the 
sixth  and   seventh    were   the   Doyles'   by   wide   margins. 

Throughout  the  match  neither  Bull  nor  Martin  approached  their  best 
form  which  was  due  in  no  small  degree  to  their  exhausting  struggle  of  the 
day  before. 

The  result  of  the  day's  play  was  to  bring  the  East  against  the  South  for 
final  honors.  The  meeting  took  place  the  following  day,  Wednesday 
before  a  crowd,  many  of  whom  expected  to  see  the  honors  fall  into  south- 
^""j  \%  ■'  ^"^,  ^"^  convincing  manner  in  which  they  had  eliminated  Bull 
and  Martin.  Though  disappointed  in  that  respect,  they  at  least  had  the 
satisfaction  of  seeing  a  match  that  abounded  in  remarkable  playing  and 
reflected  more  credit  on  C.  B.  Doyle  than  to  either  man  on  the  winning 
team.  ^ 

Paying  easily  and  almost  carelessly,  the  Easterners  went  out  in  front 
and  stayed  there  till  the  first  set  ended  at  6/4.  H.  L.  Doyle  was  the 
weak  point  and  it  was  his  service  that  was  lost  for  the  odd  game  The 
second  set  saw  the  order  of  things  reversed.  Taking  the  net  and  vo'lleving 
at  sharp  angles  and  to  their  rivals'  feet,  the  Dovles  soon  had  a  leaci  of 
four  games  to  one.  The  New  York  pair  tried  to  overcome  the  advantage 
but,  after  a  tie  at  four-all,  were  defeated  by  a  series  of  deep  lobs  and  the 
loss  of  their  position  at  the  net. 

That   setback  spurred   Touchard   and   Little   on   to   more   strenuous   efforts 
d   in   almost   no   time   the    score   in   the   third   set   stood   at   4-love   in   their 
vor.      It  was  then  the  Washingtonians'  turn  to   brace,   but  three  jjames  in 
a  row  was  their  limit,   and  the   set  finally  ended  at   6/4. 

After  the  seven-minute  interval  it  was  at  once  apparent  that  the  South- 
erners had  decided  on  a  new  plan  of  attack.  They  lobbed  continuously 
and  soon  had  Little  badly  off.  Touchard  was  no  better,  and  by  smashing 
out  and  into  the  net  they  tossed  away  numerous  points.  So  successful 
was  the  methods  of  the  brothers  that  they  evened  the  set  score  by  taking 
the  fourth  at  6/1.  •'  * 

In  spite  of  their  poor  performance  a  short  time  before,  the  Eastern  com- 
bination go  down  to  fast,  sound  play  remarkably  well.  The  fifth  set  found 
them  right  on  the  top  of  their  game  and  they  ran  up  a  lead  of  4/1  It 
was  enough  to  win  the  set  and  match,  for  the  next  four  games  were  "won 
by  the  server.

The  feature  of  the  play  was  C.  B.  Doyle's  excellent  all-round  work  His 
service  was  of  the  best  and  his  smashing  faultless.  H  E.  Doyle  s  forte 
was  his  volleying.  He  was  quick  as  a  flash  and  used  a  motion  of  the 
wrist  which  made  it  impossible  to  anticipate  which  side  of  the  court  the 
ball  would  go.  Little  and  Touchard  were  at  no  time  so  good  as  on  the 
previous  day,  although  when  points  were  most  needed  they  were  able  ta 
meet  the  emergency  successfully.  Both  were  far  ahead  of  the  Doyles  in 
ground   play,    and   it    was   that  advantage   alone   that   gave   them   the   victory.

National  Championships  for  Women 

California,  the  land  from  which  all  good  tennis  players  must  emanate, 
upheld  its  reputation  in  this  respect  when  the  challenge  round  of  the  match 
for  men  was  fought  out  by  two  experts  of  the  Golden  State,  Miss  Hazel 
Hotchkiss  and  Miss  Florence  Sutton.  The  meeting  was  the  twenty-fifth 
since  the  inauguration  of  the  event  and,  as  usual,  was  played  on  the 
Philadelphia   Cricket   Club    courts   at   Chestnut   Hill,   June    17   and   following 

By  coming  through  the  tournament  in  impressive  style  Miss  Florence 
Sutton  earned  the  privilege  of  challenging  Miss  Hotchkiss  for  the  premier 
title.  On  her  way  through,  Miss  Sutton  met  and  defeated  in  succession. 
Miss  Dorothy  Green,  6/1,  6/2;  Miss  Elizabeth  Bunce,  6/0,  6/1;  Miss 
Cheston,  6/0,  6/4;  Miss  Edna  Wildey,  6/1,  6/3;  Miss  Adelaide  Browning 
in   the   semi-final,   6/1,    6/2,  and   in   the   final   round,    Miss    Eleonora    Sears, 

From  the  recorded  scores  may  be  seen  at  a  glance  that  the  self-imposed 
task  of  disposing  of  the  other  aspirants  was  not  a  difficult  one  for  the 
California  girl.  Not  one  of  the  Eastern  experts  encountered  was  able  to 
extend  the  former  to  any  great  effort.  Miss  Sutton's  driving,  judgment 
and  consummate  skill  in  court-generaling  was  more  than  a  match  tor  the 
ambitious    Easterners.  ^  ^   ^:^ 

The 'challenge  round  will  long  be  remembered  as  one  of  the  most  excit- 
ing matches  which  ever  took  place  between  women  in  this  country.  Ihe 
play  at  the  beginning  was  somewhat  slow  and  both  players  were  inclined 
to  be  over  cautious,  consequently  there  was  no  real  form  exhibited.  Miss 
Hotchkiss  by  steadier  work  and  an  occasional  ace,  made  when  it  counted 
most,  quickly  assumed  the  lead  of  4/1.  Then  Miss  Sutton,  who  realized 
the  necessity  of  cutting  loose  from  the  careful  style  of  play  she  had  up 
to  that  time  adopted,  hit  harder  and  at  sharper  angles,  with  the  result 
that  she  quickly  brought  the  score  to  4-all  From  then  on  it  see^sawed 
back  and  forth  until  Miss  Hotchkiss  dropped  her  service  at  8-all,  and  Miss 
Sutton  won  her  own  and  the  set.  It  looked  bad  for  the  little  cham,pion 
for  Miss  Sutton  continued  her  winning  play  and  led  at  i/o  in  the  second 
set  Then  the  Berkeley  girl  demonstrated  what  resourcefulness  she  pos- 
sesses by  varving  her  game,  which  was  so  effective  that  she  ran  six 
straight  games  and  evened  the  set  score.  .  ^    ,     , 

The  third   set   found   Miss   Sutton   going  her  best.     It   was   nip  ^and  tuck

SPALDING'S    LAWN     TENNIS    ANNUAL. 


Second  Clay  Court  Championship 

Although  a  trifle  inferior  in  point  of  class  to  the  tournament  of  a  year 
ago,  the  second  clay  court  championship,  which  was  played  on  the  Omaha 
Field  Club's  courts,  beginning  August  s,  was  a  bigger  event  than  ever, 
and  in  many  ways  more  successful.  Players  from  sixteen  States  were  on 
hand,  and  a  total  entry  of  120  in  singles  and  60  teams  in  doubles  was 
recorded. 

The  one  disappointing  feature  of  the  meeting  was  the  default  of  M.  H. 
Long,  the  holder.  He  could  not  arrange  to  be  on  hand,  and  in  his  absence 
the  title  passed  on  to  W.  T.  Hayes  of  Chicago,  who  by  his  splendid  per- 
formances stamped   himself  a   worthy   successor  to   the   brilliant   Californian. 

Others  players  of  high  rank  who  participated  were  D.  Jones  of  Yale, 
H.  G.  Whitehead,  G.  O.  Winston,  the  MacQuiston  brothers,  J.  Armstrong 
and  Percy   Siverd. 

In  coming  through  Hayes  was  easily  master  in  all  his  matches.  Ray 
Branson  of  Sioux  Falls  played  well  against  him,  but  not  well  enough  to 
be  very  dangerous.  Hayes'  first  real  struggle  came  when  he  faced  Joe 
Armstrong.  For  two  sets  it  was  an  even  thing,  but  in  the  third  and 
fourth  Hayes  outclassed  the  Minnesota  champion.  The  Rev.  H.  J.  Ren- 
■dall,  by  a  persistent  net  attack,  took  two  of  the  first  three  sets  from  the 
Chicagoan.  but  his  efforts  were  so  fatiguing  that  he  could  not  continue 
them,   and   Hayes  made   short   work   of  the   remainder  of  the   match. 

On  the  other  side  of  the  draw  Siverd  had  quite  as  easy  a  time  as  Hayes 
had.  He  outclassed  all  of  his  opponents  but  two,  and  in  the  latter  cases 
was  never  in  real  danger  of  losing.  His  first  hard  match  was  with  J.  G. 
McKay  of  Indianapolis.  The  latter  was  brilliant  but  unsteady,  and  Siverd 
usually  came  out  on  top  after  struggles  for  the  advantage.  His  engage- 
ment with  J.  O.  Winston  was  a  hard  one.  Winston's  reach  made  it  hard 
for  the  Pittsburger  to  pass  him.  It  was  only  by  remarkably  clever  lob- 
bing and  then  chopping  short  that  Siverd  was  able  to  win.  His  general- 
ship was  excellent,  however,  and  he  used  his  head  in  the  manner  of  much 
older  and   more  experienced   players. 

Everyone  looked  forward  to  what  seemed  in  advance  to  be  the  match  of 
the  tournament  when  Siverd  and  Hayes  came  together,  but  there  was  a 
disappointment  in  store  for  the  spectators,  who  had  expected  a  close,  even 
fight. 

The  opening  set  was  a  bit  slow  at  first,  with  each  man  afraid  to  let  out. 
Siverd  was  very  cautious,  but  Hayes  was  puzzled  by  the  spinning  chops 
the  Pennsylvanian  sent  back  and  could  not  handle  balls  off  the  ground  at 
all  eflfectively.  At  5/4  Siverd  had  his  chance,  but  Hayes  came  in  and 
volleyed  so  well  that  three  games  went  to  him  in  rapid  succession.  It  was 
then  that  Hayes  discovered  his  opponents'  monumental  weakness,  his  back- 
hand, and  with  a  persistency  that  was  discouraging  in  the  extreme  for 
Siverd,  the  former  plugged  away  at  his  off-side.  The  result  was  a  sadly 
one-sided  victory  for  the  Chicagoan.  In  two  sets  Siverd  won  but  three 
games.  He  fought  hard  for  every  point,  but  it  was  to  no  use  or  advan- 
tage if  the  ball  shot  down  to  his  backhand.  The  return  was  invariably 
weak,  and  Hayes   was  ever   ready   for   it  at   the   net. 

The  value  o-f  experience  and  practice  of  team  work  was  effectively 
demonstrated  in  the  doubles,  for  the  pair  that  won.  Whitehead  and  Win- 
ston, have  been  partners  for  years  and  winners  of  more  than  one  big 
event.  The  latter  pair  were  drawn  in  the  upper  half,  and  after  disposmg 
of  the  Fisher  brothers  and  Siverd  and  Kendall,  both  in  straight  sets,  they 
found  themselves  opposed  to  the  McQuiston  brothers,  a  team,  like  them- 
selves,   for    years    partners   and    with    an    ability    to    work    together    equal    to 


SPALDING'S    LAWN    TENNIS    ANNUAL.  47 

their  own.  The  result  was  the  best  match  of  the  tournament,  more 
exciting  by   far   than   anything   in   either   singles   or  doubles. 

The  vigorous  hitting  of  Whitehead  and  Winston  soon  landed  them  in 
front  and  for  two  sets  it  looked  like  a  walkover.  However,  the  third  and 
fourth  sets  were  even  more  in  the  Texans'  favor  than  the  others  had  been 
against  them  and  the  fourth  was  theirs  at  love.  The  fifth  set  was  a  fight 
from  beginning  to  end.  At  one  time  the  score  stood  at  6/5  and  40/30 
for  the  brothers,  but  Whitehead  and  Winston  took  the  point  and  event- 
ually the  set  at  9/7.  It  was  as  close  a  battle  as  one  could  care  to  see, 
with  tne  losers  having  scored  two  more  games  and  two  more  points  than 
the   winners.

Intercollegiate  Championship 

The  thirty-first  intercollegiate  championships,  held  on  the  courts  of  the 
Merion  Cricket  Club,  Haverford,  Pa.,  September  7  and  following  days, 
resulted  in  a  victory  for  E.  H.  Whitney  of  Harvard  in  the  singles.  The 
Crimson's  point  total  for  the  Isaac  H.  Clothier  Trophy  is  now  six.  As 
only  seven  points  are  necessary  for  absolute  possession,  Harvard's  chances 
are  excellent.  Whtiney's  victory  over  A.  H.  Mann  of  Yale  was  achieved 
in  straight  sets,  the  score  being  6/1,  6/2,  6/2.  Dean  Mathey  and  Butler 
accounted  for  the  doubles  for  Princeton,  w'inning  from  their  college  mates, 
Titft  and    Kuhn,   by    7/5,    6/1,    6/3.      The   summary:

New  England  Intercollegiate  Tennis  Association. 

C.  L.  Johnston.  Jr.,  of  Amherst,  won  the  annual  New  England  Inter- 
collegiate championship  which  began  on  May  22  at  the  Longwood  Cricket 
Club,  Boston.  Johnston  eliminated  Oakley  of  Williams  in  the  final  round 
by  the  rather  close  score  of  8/6,  5/7,  6/3,  8/6.  Dartmouth's  champions. 
Harris  and  Nelson,  got  away  with  the  doubles.  Johnston  and  Shumway 
made  a  strong  bid  for  that  event  in  the  final,  after  winning  two  of  the 
irst  three  sets  against  the  Dartmouth  team,  but  fell  off  badly  in  the 
Fourth  and   fifth   sets.     The   summaries:

1893  at  Newport,  when  they  are  held  during  the  week  of  the  National 
Championships.

SPALDING'S    LAWN     TENNIS    ANNUAL.  57 


Lon^wood  Singles  and  Eastern  Doubles 

For  a  series  of  remarkable  happenings  and  ending  the  twenty-first  annual 
Longwood  tournament,  which  came  to  a  close  on  July  26,  has  few,  if  any, 
precedents. 

To  enumerate  all  the  unusual  incidents  of  the  week  would  require  too 
much  time  and  space.  A  number,  however,  were  the  default  of  Champion 
W.  A.  Larned;  the  winning  of  the  singles  by  his  younger  brother,  E.  P. 
Larned;  the  victorious  career  of  G.  F.  Touchard  and  R.  D.  Little  in  the 
doubles,  and  finally,  the  poor  showing  of  the  Pacific  Coast  trio  who 
participated. 

"Longwood  Week"  was,  as  usi-al,  one  of  sunshine  and  heat.  There 
was  no  bad  weather  to  mar  the  programme,  and,  as  in  years  past,  enor- 
mous crowds  turned  out  daily  to  watch  the  progress   of  play. 

There  was  but  one  bad  feature  o£  the  entire  meeting,  and  that  was  the 
extreme  unevenness  of  the  draw.  The  doubles  was  quite  well  balanced,  but 
the  former  event  was  painfully  lop-sided.  The  upper  bracket  contained 
R.  D.  Little,  W.  J.  Cothier,  N.  W.  Niles,  M.  E.  McLoughlin,  M.  H. 
Long,  T.  R.  Pell,  C.  R.  Gardner  and  G.  F.  Touchard,  while  arrayed 
against  such  talent  were  E.  P.  Larned,  L.  E.  Mahan  and  R.  Bishop  in  the 
lower  section. 

The  play  in  the  singles  was  one  unbroken  succession  of  surprises,  and 
the   doubles,   too,   was   not   without  its   thrillers. 

There  were  a  number  of  interesting  matches  in  the  first  round.  Touchard 
•was  within  a  point  of  defeat  in  his  match  against  C.  F.  Watson,  Jr.,  and 
the  veteran  R.  C.  Seaver  had  a  chance  to  demonstrate  his  ability  in  court 
covering  when  J.   O.   Ames  all   but  put  him   out. 

The  feature  of  the  second  day's  play  was  the  meeting  of  N.  W.  Niles 
and  R.  D.  Little.  Little  got  off  well  and  by  really  wonderful  driving  ran 
up  to  5/1.  Carelessness  then  cost  him  a  game  or  two,  and  when  he  tried 
to  get  back  he  found  himself  unable. 

Niles  crept  up  steadily,  and  to  the  amazement  of  everyone,  took  the 
set,  7/5.  The  second  set  was  nip  and  tuck,  with  first  one  ahead  and  then 
the  other.  Toward  the  close  Little  made  the  mistake  of  running  in,  and 
between  weak  volleying  and  being  passed  clean,  lost  two  successive  games 
and  the  match.  It  was  a  splendid  exhibition  of  base  line  play,  but  the 
net  was  severely  ostracized  until  Little  made  the  fatal  attempts  which 
ended   the  match. 

Many  fast  matches  were  played  in  the  third  round,  but  none  more  excit- 
ing than  that  between  Touchard  and  Niles,  which  was  the  morning  fea- 
ture. Niles  took  the  first  set  easily.  Touchard  was  apparently  unable  to 
get  under  way,  but  in  the  second  he  got  a  good  start  and  led  at  4/0. 
Then,  as  on  the  previous  day  against  Little,  Niles  began  an  uphill  climb 
which  ended  only  after  he  had  won  six  straight  games.  He  drove  beauti- 
fully and  continued  his  streak  until  he  led  at  5/2  and  40/15  in  the  third 
set.  There  Touchard  made  a  phenomenally  lucky  backhand  pass  and 
finally  pulled  out  the  game  and  set  at  7/5.  Improving  with  every  stroke. 
the  New  Yorker  kept  right  on,  landing  the  match  at  2/6,  4/6,  7/5,  6/3, 
6/4.  Niles  slowed  up  a  bit  toward  the  close,  being  unable  to  keep  the 
pace.     It  was  then  Touchard  had  his  chance  and  used  it. 

The  big  match  of  the  afternoon  was  the  McLoughlin-Pell  meeeting  and 
it  was  watched  by  an  enormous  crowd.  Pell,  playng  steadily  and  hitting 
hard,  had  the  first  set  won  in  jig  time.  McLoughlin  was  bad  on  ground 
strokes  and  it  was  his  inability  to  effectively  return  the  service  that  gave 
Pell  the  set  so  easily.  The  second  was  the  Californian's  by  virtue  of  a 
net  game  that  was  faultless,   but  the   third   went  to   Pell  at  6/4.      The  latter 


58  SPALDING'S    LAWN    TENNIS    ANNUAL. 

played  his  best  tennis  of  the  match  then,  passing  his  opponent  time  and 
again  for  aces.  Pell  had  every  opportunity  to  end  the  match  with  the 
fourth,  but  the  calling  of  foot  faults,  when  he  led  at  4/3,  so  demoralized 
him  that  he  lost  his  service  and  with  it  his  chance  for  the  match. 
McLoughin  took  the  set,  7/5,  and  the  fifth  at  6/1.  Pell  practically  gave 
up  in  the  last  set.  He  was  tired  and  erratic,  the  result  of  a  hard  five-set 
doubles  contest  in  the  morning. 

The  fight  between  E.  H.  Whitney  and  W.  M.  Hall  lasted  nearly  three 
and  a  half  hours.  After  winning  the  third  and  fourth  sets,  Whitney  was 
seized  with  cramps,  and  Hall  had  no  trouble  in  running  out  the  fifth  set 
at  6/2.  J.  D.  E.  Jones'  defeat  by  A.  S.  Dabney  in  four  sets  was  unex- 
pected, as  was  also  the  poor  showing  of  W.  C.  Grant  against  W.  J. 
Clothier.     Grant  was  defeated  at  6,  i,   6/1,   6/2. 

Dick  Bishop  showed  his  improved  form  by  eliminating  F.  C.  Inman  in 
a  very  even  four-set  encounter,  and  Touchard  put  young  W.  M.  Washburn 
out   of  the  running  in  a  poor  exhibition  that  lasted  all   morning. 

M.  H.  Long,  who  had  not  met  a  strong  opponent  until  he  faced  Hall, 
advanced  tn  the  fifth  round  by  winning  from  the  New  Yorker  in  straig^it 
sets.  Hair  was  good  on  all  his  strokes,  but  Long's  volleying  was  wonder- 
fully fast  and  true,  and  his  great  reach  made  it  next  to  impossible  to  pass 
him.  F.  H.  Gates  was  a  victim  of  one  of  Dabney's  "on"  days.  The  Eos- 
tonian  whanged  away  at  everything  and  rarely  failed  to  make  his  returns 
count.  Clothier  won  from  E.  Taylor  without  much  effort,  and  Mahan  and 
Larned  won   their   matches  in   straight   sets. 

The  feature  of  the  Friday's  play  was  the  encounter  of  Touchard  with 
McLoughlin.  The  latter  soon  struck  his  gait,  and  going  at  top  speed,  ran 
out  the  first  set  at  6/2.  Touchard  had  not  made  much  resistance  and 
played  indifferently  and  without  snap.  The  second  saw  McLoughlin  forg- 
ing ahead  until  he  led  at  4/2,  at  which  point,  by  placing  deep  drives  to 
the  Californian's  backhand  and  running  in,  Touchard  ran  four  straight 
games  and  the  set. 

The  third  set  was  a  runaway  for  McLoughlin,  After  falling  behind  for 
three  games,  Touchard  virtually  quit  and  made  little  effort  to  overtake 
his  rival.  The  fourth  was  Touchard's  as  easily  as  was  the  third  the  West- 
erner's. He  made  few  errors  and  forced  McLoughlin  to  volley  badly  by 
playing  slow  ones  to  his  feet.  The  fifth  was  a  struggle  that  threatened  to 
go  on  indefinitely,  not  because  the  game  score  was  big,  but  because  each 
game  in  itself  was  of  unusual  length.  Finally,  at  4/3,  Touchard  broke 
through  his  opponent's  delivery  and  after  losing  his  own,  took  the  set  and 
match  by   winning  again   on   McLoughlin's   service. 

Throughout  the  latter  part  of  the  match  Touchard  outwitted  McLoughlin 
at  every  turn.  The  latter's  insatiable  desire  to  take  the  net,  no  matter 
how  short  his  returns  might  be,  cost  him  innumerable  points,  and  his 
service,  usually  so  vicious,  was  on  that  occasion  comparatively  slow  and 
easy  to  control. 

Meanwhile  Clothier  and  Long  were  arguing  the  matter  of  superiority  on 
the  adjoining  court.  For  four  sets  it  was  a  toss-up.  Long  won  the  first 
and  third  and  Clothier  the  other  two.  Each  man  would  have  a  w-inning 
streak,  only  to  be  overhauled  and  passed  a  bit  later.  Long  was  very- 
strong  in  serving,  and  off  the  ground  Clothier  was  his  master  at  volleying 
and  overhead.  There  were  long  rallies  and  hair-raising  gets  by  both  men» 
and  up  to  the  beginning  of  the  fifth  honors  were  about   equally  divided. 

The  fifth  set  was  a  poor  finish  for  so  good  a  beginning.  The  first  three 
games  touched  deuce,  but  in  each  Long  managed  to  slip  over  the  winning- 
point.  The  second  three  were  Long's  by  wide  margins.  Clothier  mis- 
handled what  few  chances  he  had,  while  Long  was  death  to  every  ball 
within  reach.  He  cut  loose  then  and  banged  drives  and  service  aces  galore, 
and   during  the   period    was   credited   with    only   one    error. 

In  the  lower  half  Bishop  and  Dabney  clashed  and  a  five-set  match,  which 
Dabney  was  within  a  point  of  winning,  resulted.     The  latter  was  brilliant. 


SPALDING'S    LAWN    TENNIS    ANNUAL.  59 

but  at  critical  times  was  erratic.  Bishop  was  steady  always  and  at 
moments  of  pressure  capable  of  great  tennis.  Once,  when  Dabney  led  at 
two  sets  to  one  and  5/2  and  40/15,  Bishop  drove  one  so  close  that  the 
white  chalk  scattered,  and  again  Dabney  failed  to  administer  the  coup  de 
grace  when  a  rather  short  lob  was  handed  him  for  the  match  point. 

Mahan  was  way  off  his  game  when  he  faced  Larned.  He  made  a  sur- 
prising total  of  errors  and  failed  to  make  the  best  of  the  few  opportunities 
Larned  gave  him.  The  latter  played  splendidly,  driving  with  great  speed 
and  covering  his  court  in  admirable  fashion.  At  no  point  did  he  falter, 
continuing  his   pace  and  accuracy   throughout  the   three   sets. 

Perfect  weather  and  an  enthusiastic  crowd  greeted  Touchard  and  Long 
when  they  met  on  Tuesday  for  the  right  to  enter  the  final  bracket.  Long 
served  tirst  and  won  his  game  at  love,  two  points  being  service  aces 
Touchard  came  back  in  similar  fashion,  and  then  took  Long's  delivery.  At 
5/3  Long  landed  another  game,  but  the  set  ended  when  Touchard  won  his 
own  delivery  for  the  tenth. 

Touchard  increased  his  pace  with  the  second  set  and  Long  fell  oflF  badly, 
so  that  the  New  Yorker  soon  led  at  5/2.  There  Long  braced,  but  after 
winning  two  games,  dropped  his  second  set  with  the  tenth. 

The  third  was  by  far  the  best  of  the  match.  It  was  an  even  thing  to 
four-all,  at  which  point  Long  raced  ahead  to  5/4  and  40/15.  There  he 
made  two  errors  and  was  passed  twice.  Later,  at  8/7,  Long  was  three 
times   within  a  point   of   winning,   but   errors  cost  him   the  game   and  match. 

Long  was  at  no  time  going  smoothly.  He  seemed  uncertain  as  to  what 
course  to  pursue,  and  Touchard  was  quick  to  make  the  most  of  his  hesi- 
tancy. The  latter  varied  his  game  constantly,  and  it  was  that  feature 
which  Long's   indecision   was  due   to. 

In  the  lower  half  Bishop  and  Larned  played  five  long  sets  to  decide  a 
winner.  Larned  got  off  well,  winning  the  lirst  three  games,  but  Bishop 
caught  up  and  evened  the  score.  The  next  three,  with  Larned  hitting 
harder,  left  Bishop  far  behind.  Improved  volleying  and  driving  gave  the 
Bostonian  the  second  set  at  6/4.  The  third  looked  like  a  walkover  for 
the  champion's  brother.  In  three  games  he  allowed  Bishop  but  two  points 
and  later  led  at  5/2.  Here  Bishop,  taking  advantage  of  Larned's  let-down, 
came  with  a  rush  and  took  five  straight  games  and  his  second  set.  The 
loss  of  that  set  roused  Larned  and  he  went  into  the  next  with  a  grim 
determination  to  make  it  short  and  sweet.  He  was  much  too  fast  for 
Bishop  then  and  the  latter  won  only  four  more  games,  two  in  the  fourth 
and  two  in  the  fifth  sets. 

The  final  between  Larned  and  Touchard  was  played  before  a  record- 
breaking  gallery.  In  view  of  his  success  in  the  earlier  rounds,  the  odds 
seemed  to  favor  Touchard's  winning.  That  he  failed  and  was  outplayed, 
actually  outclassed,  came  as  a  surprise  to  the  majority  of  the  game's  fol- 
lowers. Larned  won  in  four  sets  and  at  no  time  was  in  real  danger.  His 
tennis  was  of  a  kind  to  inspire  all  who  saw  it  and  there  was  no  flaw  or  a 
criticism  to  make  from  beginning  to  end.  No  one  could  have  played  with 
a  greater  degree  of  accuracy  and  pace  combined,  nor  could  anyone  have 
conceived  a  better  plan  of  attack.  Following  terrfiic  drives  into  the  net, 
there  to  kill  with  a  precision  that  was  masterly,  was  his  plan  of  campaign, 
from  which  he  never  deviated.  Touchard  never  had  a  chance  to  really 
start,  for  his  returns  were  smothered  and  he  was  on  the  defensive  nine- 
tenths  of  the  time.  The  match  lasted  a  little  over  an  hour  and  both  men 
were  as  fresh   at  the  finish  as  at  the   beginning  of   play. 

W.  A.  Larned,  who  for  years  past  has  won  the  Longwood  event,  claimed 
the  right  to  default  to  his  brother.  Ned,  and  after  the  latter's  perform- 
ance, there  were  few  to  question  his  ability  to  guard  the  title  thus  thrust 
upon  him. 

No  less  surprising  was  the  result  of  the  doubles  event  than  the  singles 
had  been,  for  out  of  the  mass  of  teams  which  started  off  in  the  preliminary 
round  was  to  emerge  a  pair  who,  though  they  played  together  for  the  first 


60  SPALDING'S    LAWN    TENNIS    ANNUAL. 

time  at  Longwood.  were  to  finally  accomplish  the  greatest  feat  that  a  team 
can,  namely,  overthrow  the  national  champions  at  Newport  and  earn  the 
premier   honors  in   the   doubles  game. 

There  was  plenty  of  "class"  to  the  combination  entered.  Larned  and 
Clothier,  Gardner  and  Long,  Wright  and  Xiles,  Pell  and  Mahan  and 
Touchard  and  Little  were  formemost  among  them,  and  there  were  plenty 
of   fast   matches   each   day  of   play. 

The  first  round  produced  only  one  contest  of  note,  and  that  was  between 
Clothier  and  Larned  and  C.  F.  Watson,  Jr.,  and  H.  Torrance.  The  former 
pair  ran  out  the  first  two  sets  easily  at  6/2,  6/2,  but  the  third  and  fourth 
were  as  close  as  one  could   wish   to  see. 

The  second  round  saw  the  near  defeat  of  Touchard  and  Little  by  G.  P. 
Gardner  and  A.  S.  Dabney.  The  latter  pair  led  at  two  sets  to  one  and 
3/1  before  the  New  Yorkers  pulled  together  and  drew  away.  Pell  and 
Mahan  had  hard  work  to  dispose  of  E.  P.  Larned  and  F.  T.  Freyling- 
huysen,  but  the  rest  of  the  big  men  came  through   with  comparative  ease. 

The  third  round  produced  nothing  startling,  but  in  the  fourth  there  were 
two  contests  that  were  spectacular  struggles  from  their  very  beginning. 
The  featured  one  was  that  between  Touchard  and  Little  and  Gardner  and 
Long.  The  first  set  went  to  Long  and  Gardner  at  7/5.  The  team  work 
of  their  opponents  was  a  sad  afifair.  Touchard  was  the  offender,  being 
slow  to  run  in,  and  covering  only  a  small  part  of  the  court  that  was  his 
to   look  after. 

The  second  and  third  sets  were  faster  and  in  favor  of  the  Easterners. 
By  dint  of  many  expressive  suggestions  and  rules  made  then  and  there  by 
Little,  the  head  of  the  combination,  Touchard  woke  up  and  worked  much 
harder.  The  fourth  set  was  won  by  Long  and  Gardner  quite  easily,  and 
they  continued  to  play  all  around  the  New  Yorkers  until  the  score  stood 
at  5/2  and  40/0,  or  match  point,  in  their  favor.  Touchard  was  serving, 
and,  as  all  through  the  tournament,  his  service  was  weak  and  uncertain, 
but  a  smash  of  Little's  and  two  errors  of  the  Californians  evened  the 
score.  From  that  moment  on  the  latter  team  went  off  and  by  fast,  hard 
tennis  Touchard  and   Little  took  four  straight   games  and   the   match. 

The  second  important  match  of  the  day  was  that  between  Niles  and 
Wright  and  Pell  and  Mahan.  It  was  played  on  a  court  badly  cut  up  and 
in  need  of  attention,  and  the  false  bounds  were  numerous.  The  tennis 
was,  as  a  consequence,  much  below  standard.  Not  one  of  the  four  was 
in  good  form,  Wright,  in  particular,  being  off  on  all  his  strokes.  The  first 
two  sets  went  to  the  Bostonians  and  the  third  and  fourth  to  Pell  and 
Mahan.  The  fifth,  which  was  played  on  another  court  in  somewhat  better 
condition,  went  to  the  Hub  players  at  6/3.  Niles  was  the  best  of  the 
four,  being  good  at  driving  and  serving.  Pell  and  Mahan  had  alternate 
streaks  of  good  and  bad  playing,  but  not  once  could  they  both  settle  down 
in  unison. 

The  remaining  two  pairs  to  come  through  were  Larned  and  Clothier, 
who  took  the  measure  of  Putnam  and  Seaver  in  straight  sets,  and  Bishop 
and   Whitney,   who   vanquished   Grant  and    McKinney. 

On  Saturday  afternoon  Larned  and  Clothier  and  Touchard  and  Little 
furnished  the  excitement  for  the  day.  The  match  abounded  in  fast,  clean 
hitting,  but  neither  pair  was  up  to  the  mark  in  team  work.  Little  and 
Touchard  showed  a  vast  improvement,  but  also  a  lack  of  finish  that  made 
their  game  seem  very  ragged.  Larned  and  Clothier  were  far  worse  in  that 
respect,  clashing  repeatedly,  and  often  leaving  one  side  of  their  court 
exposed.  Clothier  as  unsteady  and  soft,  double-faulting  frequently  and 
volleying  poorly  in  each  set.  Larned  was  very  good  on  strokes  of  all 
kinds,  but  seemed  to  have   little  idea   of  position   or  defense. 

The  final,  played  on  Monday,  attracted  an  unusually  large  attendance  in 
spite  of  the  weather,  which  threatened  showers  momentarily  throughout 
the  afternoon. 

The   first   set  was  even   up  to  four-all,    at   which   point   Niles  dropped   his 


SPALDING'S    LAWN    TENNIS    ANNUAL.  61 

service  and  Touchard  won  his.  The  second  was  precisely  the  same,  except 
that  at  four-all  Wright  weakened  and  failed  to  win  his  delivery,  and  Little 
served   out  the   tenth   game. 

The  New  Yorkers  kept  on  going  until,  with  Touchard  serving,  at  5/4 
and  30-Iove,  a  ridiculous  thing  occurred.  Eight  successive  times  the  service 
was  delivered,  and  not  once  did  the  ball  tind  the  court.  Four  double- 
faults  and  a  game  thrown  away.  It  was  unbelievable  that  such  a  thing 
could  happen.  However,  the  check  thus  occasioned  was  temporary,  for  the 
New  York  combination  broke  Niles'  service  and  won  Little's  for  the 
following  two   games. 

As  a  team,  the  Bostonians  proved  disappointing.  They  did  not  work 
well  together  nor  individually.  Wright  was  better  than  at  any  previous 
time  in  the  week,  but  Niles  was  way  off  form.  He  volleyed  wretchedly 
.and  his  usually  strong  service  was  noticeable  only  for  its  weakness.  The 
summaries;

SPALDING'S    LAWN    TENNIS    ANNUAL.  63 

New  England  Championship 

R.  A.  Holden,  Jr.  covered  himself  with  glory  by  coming  through  the 
field  at  the  annual  tournament  for  the  New  England  championship,  which 
was   begun   at   the   Hartford    (Conn.)    Golf   Club   on   June    19. 

In  the  challenge  round  Holden  met  T.  R.  Pell,  the  title-holder  for  four 
years,  and  defeated  him  in  five  sets.  Pell  at  one  time  led  at  two  sets  to 
one,   but  Holden  won  the   fourth  at   6/4  and  the  fifth   easily  at   6/2. 

F.  H.  Harris,  the  Dartmouth  player  who  has  come  on  so  rapidly,  was 
Holden's  opponent  in  the  final.  The  first  two  sets  were  divided,  but  the 
third  and  fourth  both  went  to  "Rube"  at  6/2.  His  easy,  graceful  style 
was  a  pleasure  to  watch,  and  no  matter  how  difficult  the  returns  he  was 
called  upon  to  make  might  be,  he  never,  apparently,  exerted  himself  to 
any   great   degree. 

In  tlie  doubles  Harris  had  tetter  luck,  for,  paired  with  J.  G.  Nelson, 
he  defeated,  in  a  long  four-set..match,  W.  C.  Grant  and  H.  A.  McKinney. 
The  sum.maries:  "•  . 

SINGLES.

SPALDING'S    LAWN    TENNIS    ANNUAL.  65 

Middle  States  Championship 

The  annual  tournament  for  the  championship  of  the  Middle  States  was 
begun  on  July  2  at  Orange,  N.  J.,  but  failed  to  attract  the  high  class 
men  that  for  years  past  have  competed,  and  was  also  below  the  records  ot 
previous  years  in  the   number   of  entries. 

To  add  to  disappointment,  the  challenge  match  between  W.  M.  Hall 
and  the  holder.  N.  W.  Niles,  came  to  an  abrupt  end  when  Niles  twisted 
his  ankle  and  as  a  result   was  forced   to  default. 

"Hall  in  winning  the  tournament  put  Dean  Mathey  and  R.  A.  Holden 
out  of  the  running.  Holden,  who  had  previously  defeated  E.  P.  Larned 
in  a  pretty  three-set  battle,  was  vanquished  by  Hall  at  6/4,  7/5,  9/7.  Hall 
was  the  harder  and  steadier  and  played  his  best  when  in  the  deepest  holes. 

To  reach  the  final.  Hall  had  for  an  opponent  in  the  semi-final  Dean 
Mathey.  The  Princeton  champion  played  all  around  Hall  for  a  time  and 
was  once  within  a  point  of  victory.  He  lacked  the  steadiness  at  that 
stage  to  bring  off  the  winning  return  and  was  never  dangerous  after  the 
third    set  began. 

The  challenge  match  while  it  lasted  seemed  an  even  thing.  Niles  won 
the  first  set  at  9/7  by  better  driving  toward  its  finish.  The  second  set 
stood  at  4/5  and  40/15,  with  Niles  serving,  when  he  suddenly  wrenched 
his   foot  in   running  back  and  was  forced  to  drop  out. 

The  doubles  attracted  twenty-one  teams.  The  biggest  surprise  of  the 
week  came  with  the  defeat  of  Clothier  and  E.  P.  Larned  by  the  Gates 
brothers  of  Yale.  It  occurred  in  the  first  round  to  the  tune  of  2/6,  6/3, 
8/6.  Bull  and  Martin  found  C.  F.  Watson,  Jr.  and  F.  Souther  a  good 
team,  but  after  losing  the   first   set  at  8/6,   took   the  match   at   6/3,   6/2. 

In  the  semi-finals  Torrance  and  Hinck,  a  veteran  combination  with  excel- 
lent team  work,  proved  too  experienced  for  the  Gates,  whom  they  defeated 
at  6/4,  6/2.  Holden  and  Mathey  were  no  match  for  Bull  and  Martin  and 
lost  at  6/3,   6/3. 

The  final  developed  a  big  surprise  in  the  straight  set  defeat  of  the 
Crescent  club  combination  by  liinch  and  Torrance.  Only  the  first  set  was 
close,  and  that  went  to  the  winners  at  6/4.  The  second  and  third  were 
very   one-sided,    for   Bull   and    Martin   won  but   a  singles   game   in   each. 

The  challenge  match  was  as  much  in  favor  of  the  holders,  Hackett  and 
Alexander,  as  the  final  had  been  for  Hinck  and  Torrance.  The  defending 
team  was  too  fast  and  aggressive  for  the  title  seekers  and  won  at  6/3, 
6/2,   6/4.      The   summaries:

Western  Championship 

By  far  the  biggest  and  best  tournament,  from  every  standpoint,  that  has 
"been  held  in  the  West  was  that  for  the  Western  Championship,  which  was 
begun  on  July  22  at  the  Onwentsia  Club  of  Lake  Forest,  a  suburb  of 
Chicago. 

Everything — courts,  weather  and  tournament  management — was  ideal 
from  the  players'  standpoint,  and  the  hospitality  and  entertainment  afforded 
the  visitors,  who  came  from  Maine  to  California,  made  their  stay  a 
delightful  one. 

The  first  match  of  much  importance  was  that  between  McLoughlin  and 
J.  H.  Winston  in  the  second  round.  Winston  was  at  his  best  and  forced 
the  Californian  to  a  deuce  set,  but  was  outclassed  in  the  second.  H.  H. 
Whitman,  brother  to  the  ex-champion,  defeated  Drummond  Jones  with 
surprising  ease   in  the   same   round. 

The  third  round  brought  together  C.  M.  Bull,  Jr.,  who,  with  Martin, 
had  made  the  journey  from  New  York  with  their  eyes  on  the  doubles,  and 
Heath  Byford.  The  latter  played  splendidly,  and  Bull  just  got  by  in  a 
close  third  set.  Waidner,  a  star  for  ten  years  past,  eliminated  Whitehead 
by  6/4,  6/j,  and  H.  C.  Martin  dropped,  by  a  similar  score,  his  chances  to 
Walter  Hayes. 

G.  M.  Church  had  an  awful  time  with  Albrecht  Ludke.  Ludke  was  hit- 
ting much  too  hard  for  the  Princetonian  at  first  and  won  at  6/3.  The 
second  was  Church's  by  6/1.  Each  man  was  within  a  point  of  the  third 
before   Church   finally   captured   it   at   9/7. 

The  feature  of  the  fourth  round  was  the  meeting  of  L.  H.  Waidner  and 
W.  T.  Hayes.  The  two  have  met  often  and  the  results  have  invariably 
favored  Waidner.  Hayes  turned  the  tables,  however,  and  outplayed  his 
famous  opponent  two  to  one.  The  score  was  6/4,  6/1.  Bull  rather  unex- 
pectedly eliminated  C.  B.  Doyle  in  straight  sets,  and  McLoughlin  held  J. 
Armstrong  safe  in  the  same  round.  H.  H.  Whitman  advanced  a  round 
through  the  default  of   Church. 

The  semi-finals  brought  together  Bull  and  Hayes  and  Whitman  and 
McLoughlin.  Bull  led  Hayes  at  two  sets  to  one  when  darkness  ended 
hostilities.  The  next  day  he  defaulted  in  order  to  save  himself  for  the 
doubles.  Against  McLoughlin,  Whitman  failed  to  ^make  much  headway  and 
was  defeated  at  6/4,  6/4,   6/1. 

The  final,  played  on  Saturday  between  Hayes  and  McLoughlin,  was  all 
in  favor  of  the  Californian.  Hayes  was  baffled  by  the  Westerner's  speed 
and  could  do  nothing  with  his  service,  so  that  the  result  was  never  for  a 
moment  in  doubt.     The  score  was  6/2,  6/3,  6/3. 

The  challenge  round  between  McLoughlin  and  Bundy,  the  holder,  was 
fast  and  interesting  from  start  to  finish,  although  the  advantage,  after  the 
first  set,  was  all  McLoughlin's.  He  improved  rapidly  and  was  compara- 
tively little  bothered  by  a  strong  wind  which  blew  across  the  court. 
Bundy's  slower  chops  were  carried  all  over  and  he  lost  control  of  his 
ground  returns  completely.  McLoughlin  was  so  much  faster  that  the  match 
took  on  a  very  one-sided  aspect  as  it  progressed.  The  finish  was  tame  and 
flat.  Bundy,  apparently  realizing  the  futility  of  his  efforts,  did  not  make 
the  determined  stand  it  was  thought  he  might,  and  the  match  ended  in 
McLoughlin's  favor  at  4/6,  6/1,  6/3,  6/3. 

The  real  interest  in  the  tournament  was  aroused  in  the  struggle  for 
doubles  honors.  The  Eastern  event  had  falj.en  to  Touchard  and  Little, 
the  Southern  to  the  Doyle  brothers  of  WasPfifigton,  and  the  premier  pair 
on  the  Coast  had  been  decided  when  McLoughlin  and  Bundy  had  taken 
the   measures   of  all  teams  who   had  questioned   their   superiority. 


McI.OTGnLIN. 


SPALDING'S    LAWN    TENNIS    ANNUAL.  €9 

The  Western  title  was  the  last  decided  and  the  remaining  one  to  create 
the  play-off  for  the  privilege  of  playing  the  champions,  Hackett  and 
Alexander,  at  Newport.  The  entry,  accordingly,  was  a  large  one.  Most 
of  the  teams  were  of  men  who  resided  in  the  West,  but  there  were  others 
from  widely  distant  sections,  namely:  P>u]l  and  Martin  and  Church  and 
Mathey   from   New   York,  and  Whitehead  and   Winston   from   Norfolk. 

In  the  first  round  Church  and  Mathey  were  pushed  to  five  sets  by  A.  L. 
Green  and  A.  Squair,  and  Whitehead  and  Winston  -dropped  a  set  to  Neely 
and  Hayes.  The  Princetonians  got  better  as  they  went  along  and,  dispos- 
ing of  Waidner  and   Whitman,   landed  in  the  final. 

In  the  other  half  Bull  and  Martin  got  by  their  opponents  one  after 
another  until  a  four-set  victory  from  the  men  from  Norfolk  gave  them  the 
right  to   fight  it  out  with  Church  and   Mathey. 

The  final  match  was  a  heart-breaking  five-set  affair,  which  hung  in  the 
balance  until  the  last  ball  crossed  the  net.  The  first  two  sets  went  to  the 
Princetonians  and  the  third  to  Bull  and  Martin.  The  fourth  saw  the 
youngsters  at  their  best  and  they  piled  up  a  lead  of  5/1.  The  seventh 
game,  with  Church  serving,  went  to  40/15  before  Bull  and  Martin  began 
to  pick  up.  Steadily,  point  by  point,  they  came  on  until  with  a  rally  that 
was  remarkable  for  its  aggressive  force  and  accuracy,  they  drew  even  and 
ran  out  two  games  for  the  set.  The  crowd  shouted  its  approval  and  the 
encouragement  acted  like  a  tonic  on  the  tired  Crescent  men.  Bull,  in 
spite  of  his  powerful  appearance,  was  all  but  exhausted,  and  at  that  time 
it   seemed  doubtful   if   he   could   continue. 

Though  discouraged,  as  the  youngsters  must  have  been  by  such  a  won- 
derful uphill  fight  of  their  opponents,  they  did  not  show  it,  and  the  fifth 
set  was  much  like  its  predecessor.  The  stand  this  time  was  made  by 
Mathey  and  Church,  and  loud  applause  greeted  their  winning  of  the  game, 
which  made  the  score  five-all.  Finally,  however,  at  seven-all,  Bull  and 
Martin  made  a  last  stand,  and  it  succeeded.  They  took  two  games  and 
the  match,  as  hard  fought  and  even  a  one  as  was  ever  plaryed  in  the  West. 

The  entry  of  Miss  Hazel  Hotchkiss  stirred  up  unusual  interest  in  the 
events  for  women.  That  she  won  her  matches  easily  was  but  fulfilling 
expectations.  Her  opponent  in  the  final  was  Miss  Carrie  B.  Neely.  Miss 
Neely   was  outclassed  and   won   only   a   single  game  in   each  set. 

Playing  together  in  doubles,  Miss  Hotchkiss  and  Miss  Neely  captured 
the  event  with  no  serious  opposition,  except  the  stand,  made  by  Miss  Marie 
Wimer  and  Miss  Hoyt.  In  that  match  an  odd  set  was  required,  but  in 
the  final,  against  Miss  Adelaide  Chatfield-Taylor  and  Miss  Marion  Butler, 
the  winners  ran   out  at   6/2   and  6/3. 

Miss  Hotchkiss  scored  the  third  victory  when  playing  with  M.  E. 
McLoughlin.  She  ran  off  with  the  mixed  event.  In  the  final  Miss  Neely 
and   L.   H.   Waidner   were   their  victims   by   a   6/2-,   6/3   score.

SPALDING'S    LAWN    TENNIS    ANNUAL.  73 


Pacific  Coast  Championship 

M.  E.  McLoughlin's  third  win  of  the  season  came  on  June  26,  when,  at 
Santa  Cruz,  on  the  courts  of  the  Casa  del  Rey  Hotel,  he  became  coast 
champion  for  the  third  time.  The  tournament  attracted  all  the  best  of  the 
Northern  California  experts  and  many  from  the  southern  part  of  the 
State.  Seven  events  were  run  off — men's  singles  and  doubles,  women's 
singles  and  doubles,  mixed  doubles  and  a  junior  singles  and  doubles  for 
men. 

In  the  men's  singles  McLoughlin  and  Foley  clashed  in  the  final.  The 
match  was  fast  and  interesting,  but  McLoughlin  held  it  safe  at  all  times, 
winning    at    6/2,    6/3,    6/2. 

The  challenge  round  between  McLoughlin  and  Long  was  as  uneven  in 
the  game  score  as  the  final  had  been,  but  the  tennis  was  of  a  much  higher 
standard.  Long,  though  not  at  his  best,  was  good  and  the  rallies  were 
frequent   and    brilliant. 

The  doubles  did  not  involve  a  title  as  the  singles  had,  and  was  therefore 
of  less  interest.  Nevertheless  there  was  plenty  of  excellent  tennis  in  every 
round.  Foley  and  Long  won  from  W.  Mace  and  W.  Dawson  at  6/2,  8/10, 
4/6,   6/1,  6/1    in  the   final. 

Miss  May  Sutton  had  no  great  difficulty  in  holding  her  title  against 
Miss  Browne's  efforts  to  snatch  it  away.  The  two  sets  played  were  fast 
and  full  of  long  rallies,  but  Miss  Sutton's  control  was  better  than  her 
opponent's   and   her  shots    had   greater   pace.      The   summaries:

SPALDING'S   LAWN    TENNIS    ANNUAL.  75 

Pacific  Coast  Doubles. 

A  great  entry  was  received  for  the  doubles  championship  of  the  Pacific 
Coach,   which   began  July   i.   at   Long   Beach,   just   out  of   Los  Angeles. 

All  the  big  men  from  Northern  and  Southern  California  took  part,  but 
the  final  result,  with  a  man  from  the  eastern  section  comprising  the  suc- 
cessful team,  must  have  done  much  to  alleviate  the  feeling  so  pronounced 
between  the  two  sections.  T.  C.  Bundy  from  Los  Angeles  and  M.  E. 
McLoughlin  from  San  Francisco  were  the  triumphant  combination,  and 
their   victory  was  as  decisive  as  it  was  popular. 

Their  first  match  was  an  easy  one  and  the  second  was  but  little  harder. 
In  the  latter  their  opponents,  R.  G.  Hufit  and  F.  Knowlton,  were  good 
enough   to   extend    lliem.    but   there    was   never   a   doubtful    moment. 

The  semi-final  brought  the  winners  against  Hal  Braly  and  T.  Hendrick, 
Bundy's  partner  of  the  previous  yar.  McLoughlin  and  Braly  were  the 
stars  of  their  respective  sides,  each  hitting  with  tremendous  power  and 
quite  remarkable  control.  It  was  an  exciting  battle  from  beginning  to  end 
and   was   won   at   6/4.   6/4.  4/6.   6/4. 

The  second  semi-final  match  was  between  Foley  and  Hardy  and  Browne 
and   Duncan.      It  went  the   full  five  sets   before  the   San   Franciscans   won. 

The  final  was  a  thrilling,  spectacular  battle  that  attracted  an  enormous 
and  appreciative  crowd.  The  first  set  was  one  of  the  longest  in  Coast  rec- 
ords. It  was  an  even  thing  to  four-all.  at  which  point  the  server  won 
until  14-all  was  reached.  At  that  point  Foley's  delivery  was  won  by 
Bundy  and  McLoughlin.  and  the  set  went  to  the  pair  with  the  next  game. 
Foley's  play  was  the  best  of  the  four  during  that  set.  His  smashing  was 
wonderful,  and  no  more  speedy  or  accurate  driving  has  ever  been  seen  on 
the  Coast.  The  second  set,  in  which  Foley  continued  to  outplay  his  part- 
ner and  opponents,  went  to  him  and  Hardy  at  6/4.  The  third  and  fourth 
sets  were  like  the  other  two,  principally  on  account  of  their  difference. 
Foley  seemed  worn  and  steadily  slowed  down.  Hardy,  too,  showed  signs 
of  fatigue,  but  their  opponents  were  faster  than  before.  The  result  was 
that   Bundy  and   McLoughlin   ran  out  at   6/1,   6/1.      The   summaries:

76  SPALDING'S    LAWN     TENNIS     ANNUAL. 


Southampton  Tournament 

For  the  second  time  in  two  years,  the  "California  Peril"  laid  a  devas- 
tating course  through  the  Eastern  talent  arrayed  against  it  at  Southamp- 
ton's big  meeting,  which  began  on  the  famous  Meadow  Club's  courts, 
August  14.  The  invasion  was  of  even  greater  fatality  to  the  hopes  of 
some  of  the  Eastern  "ten,"  for  both  first  and  second  places  fell  to  the 
men  from  the  coast.  The  entry  was  an  exceptionally  high  class  one  and 
included    Behr   and    Clothier,   as   well   as   Long,    Bundy,   Pell   and    others. 

The  first  round  brought  together  Behr  and  Clothier,  but  after  a  few 
games  had  been  played  a  storm  put  an  end  to  hostilities.  The  following 
day  Clothier  increased  his  4/2  lead  to  6/2,  but  Behr  improved  rapidly. 
Winning  a  close  second  set  he  made  short  work  of  the  third,  taking  the 
match  at  2/6,  7/s,  6/3.  M.  H.  Long  had  no  easy  time  winning  from 
Schwengers,  the  Victorian  champion,  these  two  matches  being  the  fea- 
tures  of  the   round. 

In  the  second  round  Behr  was  perilously  near  defeat  in  his  match  with 
J.  D.  E.  Jones,  but  managed  to  pull  out  by  some  miraculous  gets  toward 
the  end. 

The  third  was  exciting  because  of  the  Pell-Niles  meeting.  Pell  won  in 
straight  sets  by  the  cleanest  sort  of  fast,  sound  tennis.  Behr  eliminated 
W.  F.  Johnson,  and  Bundy  took  L.  E.  Mahan  into  camp  in  the  ssme 
round. 

The  semi-final  brackets  brought  out  two  of  the  best  matches  of  the 
week.  Behr  was  coupled  with  Bundy  and  Long  found  himself  opposed  to 
Pell.  The  former  match  was  by  far  the  better.  Behr  wasted  no  time  in 
getting  under  way  and  after  establishing  a  big  lead  took  the  first  set,  6/4, 
Still  going  strongly,  the  New  Yorker  got  to  5/4,  but  luck  was  against 
him  and  he  faded  from  that  time  on.  Bundy  got  the  set  at  8/6,  and  in 
the  third  his  clever  playing  so  tired  his  opponent  that  the  latter  volun- 
tarily   stopped    half    way    to    the    finish. 

The  Pell-Long  match  was  closer  than  the  other  had  been.  The  former 
finally  won  the  opening  set,  10/8,  but  Long  was  always  ahead  in  the 
second.  The  third  provided  the  visitor  with  an  awful  scare.  By  careful, 
hard  work  Pell  got  to  4/1,  only  to  have  Long  catch  him.  The  ninth 
game  was  Pell's,  due  to  Long's  want  of  steadiness,  but  it  was  his  last. 
He  became  very  erratic  for  no  apparent  reason  and  Long  took  three 
games   without   trouble. 

The  final  match  between  Bundy  and  Long  was  played  in  a  high  wind, 
which  interfered  greatly  with  the  accuracy  of  both  men.  Bundy  found 
himself  first,  and  while  Long  was  still  experimenting  with  the  wind 
carry,  etc.,  ran  out  the  set  at  6/3.  Bundy  had  completely  mastered  the 
situation  by  the  time  the  second  set  commenced,  and  with  an  exhibition 
of  marvellous  accuracy  and  judgment  ran  six  straight  games.  The  little 
expert  slowed  up  a  bit  then  and  Long  was  quick  to  take  advantage  of 
the  action.  He  came  in  at  every  opportunity  and  by  much  improved  vol- 
leying  took    si.x   01    the    nine    games   played. 

The  fourth  was  fast  and  hotly  contested.  Long  seemed  a  trifle  more 
effective  and  led  at  5/4  and  40-love.  Bundy  placed  perfectly  then  but 
though  he  evened  the  game  score  he  was  outdistancd  in  his  race  for  the 
following  two. 

The  fifth  set  was  of  short  duration.  Bundy  fought  like  a  demon  for 
every  point  and,  though  every  game  touched  deuce,  Long  managed  to 
land   but    one. 

The  sensation  of  the  tournament  was  provided  by  the  doubles  event. 
Bundy   and   McLoughlin   were   undoubtedly   the   logical   team   for   first   place, 


SPALDING'S    LAWN     TENNIS     ANNUAL.  77 

but,  to  the  consternation  of  their  backers,  went  down  before  the  remark- 
ably line  game  of  R.  Fincke  and  C.  F.  Watson,  Jr.  It  was  a  close  three- 
set  afifair,  but  at  four-all  in  the  third  the  Easterner  braced  and  by  faultless 
tennis    won    the    necessary    pair    of   games. 

The  final  round  brought  Fincke  and  Watson  and  the  Larned  brothers 
to  opposite  sides  of  the  net.  The  match  was  fast  and  quite  even  and 
went  the  limit  of  live  sets  before  the  Larneds  emerged  triumphant.  W.  A. 
Larned  was  the  star  of  the  four  and  it  was  largely  due  to  his  perfect 
ground   strokes   that  he   and   E.   P.    were  able   to   win. 

Miss  Marion  Fenno  and  Fincke  had  the  mixed  doubles  event  won  from 
the  start.  No  other  team  approached  them,  and  in  the  final  Miss  Hen- 
derson  and   C.    F.    Watson   were   decisively   defeated   at   6/0   and   6/4. 

The    summaries:

SPALDING'S    LAWN    TENNIS    ANNUAL.  79 


Sectional  Championships 


Gulf  States  Championship. 

That  the  South  is  becoming  more  and  more  an  important  factor  in 
American  tennis  was  evidenced  by  the  large  crowd  and  high  class  tour- 
nament which  was  played  during  the  week  of  June  19,  at  the  New  Orleans 
Tennis  Club,  for  the   Gulf   States  championship. 

The  entry  was  an  excellent  one,  including  L.  H.  Waidner  of  Chicago, 
a  player  of  international  reputation;  W.  F.  Johnson  of  Philadelphia,  and 
H.  M.   McQuiston  of  Texas. 

The  semi-final  brackets  were  filled  by  H.  McQuiston  and  Johnson  in  the 
upper  half  and  by  Waidner  and  Phelps  in  the  lower.  The  latter  proved 
to  be  no  match  for  Waidner  at  all,  but  the  Johnson-McQuiston  set-to  was 
a  hummer.  The  Philadelphian  won  the  opening  set  at  6/4,  but  after  drop- 
ping far  behind  in  the  second,  gave  it  up  and  allowed  it  to  go  at  love. 
The  third  was  a  base  line  battle,  in  which  each  man  won  the  other's 
service  up  to  three-all.  At  that  point  McQuiston  took  a  game  by  smashing 
and  another  by  getting  three  successive  net  cord  shots,  not  one  of  which 
Johnson  could  reach.  He  won  the  set  and  match  with  the  ninth  game  by 
clever  serving  to  Johnson's  backhand. 

That  brought  Waidner  and  McQuiston  together  in  the  final,  and  in  spite 
of  the  belief  that  a  close  match  would  result,  Waidner  won  easily  in  three 
sets.  He  hit  much  harder  and  volleyed  at  better  angles.  McQuiston,  on 
the  other  hand,  was  soft  and  rather  unsteady  and  far  below  his  best  form. 

The  challenge  match  resulted  in  another  victory  for  Waidner.  The  1910 
champion,  Paul  McQuiston,  had  no  better  luck  than  his  brother,  although 
he  came  within  a  point  of  winning  the  first  set.  On  account  of  the  late 
hour  at  which  play  was  commenced,  it  was  decided  to  play  only  two  out 
of  three  sets.     Waidner  won  at  9/7,   6/3. 

The  doubles  event  resulted  in  a  great  five-set  final  match  between  Waid- 
ner and  Johnson  and  the  McQuiston  brothers.  In  the  end  the  long 
experience  of  playing  together  proved  enough  to  give  the  brothers  the  win 
at  6/2,  6/1,   s/7,  6/2,  6/3.     The   summaries: 

MEN'S  DOUBLES. 

Preliminary  round — Legendre  and  Warriner  d.  De  Buys  and  Hender-

Northwestern  Championship. 

L.  Harry  Waidner  successfully  defended  his  title  of  Northwestern 
champion,  which  was  held  on  the  Minnetonka  Yacht  Club  courts  at  Lake 
Minnetonka,  Minn.  It  was  Waidner's  fourth  year  of  title  holding  and  it 
was  secured  by  a  victory  over  J.  Adams,  the  Minnesota  champion.  Adams 
met  an  old  rival  in  the  final  round  when  he  faced  Paul  Armstrong  and  a 
stubborn  battle  was  waged  before  the  former  pulled  out.  The  score  was 
8/6,    4/6,    6/4,    10/8. 

In  the  challenge  round  Waidner  proved  his  right  to  the  premier  honors 
by  disposing  of  Adams  at  6/2,   7/5,  3/6,   6/3. 

Adams  and  Armstrong  won  the  doubles.  Their  only  hard  match  was 
the  final  against  Ward  Burton  and  L.  H.  Northrop.  The  latter  pair  were 
too  erratic  to  be  effective  at  critical  times.  They  would  succeed  in  a  game 
or  two  of  really  excellent  play,  but  then  came  a  streak  of  wildness  which 
more  than   offset  their   good   work.      The   summaries:

Central  United  States  Championship. 

Tennis  in  St.  Louis  is  mainly  fostered  by  the  Triple  A  Club.  They  are 
recognized  as  the  only  tennis  club  in  the  city,  but  being  practically  the 
only  club  active  in  the  game  of  tennis,  by  no  means  can  it  be  said  that 
they  do  not  encourage  the  game.  They  have  eighteen  of  the  finest  clay 
courts  in  the  country,  which  are  always  in  the  best  of  condition,  every 
court  being  occupied  from  morning  to  twilight  during  the  season.  They 
extend  to  the  wives  and  daughters  of  its  members  all  the  privileges  of  the 
courts  in  the  mornings  and  the  boys  under  eighteen  years  have  free  use 
of  same.  It  is  not  an  uncommon  sight  to  see  the  future  tennis  players, 
ranging  in  age  from  seven  years  up,  playing  in  the  foreno6ns. 

During  the  season  the  club  gives  three  tournaments  open  to  its  mem- 
bers, namely,  the  Inaugural,  in  April;  Mid-Summer,  in  June,  and  the  club 
championship   in   August. 

In  July  the  Central  championship  is  held  under  the  auspices  of  the 
club;  in  September,  the  city  championship  for  women  and  men.  The  inter- 
scholastic  and  the  Sunday  School  championships  of  the  city  are  also  staged. 

The  Central  championship  is  the  big  event  of  the  season  and  attracts  a 
large  entry,  owing  to  the  hospitality  and  fine  prizes  offered.  It  is  one  of 
the  big  events  of  the  country.  Over  one  thousand  dollars  are  presented 
to  the  winners  in  cups  and  the  visitors  are  entertained  at  the  club  house 
while  in  the  city,  and  at  the  end  of  the  tournament  a  banquet  is  given 
them  at  one   of  the  leading  summer  gardens. 

In  191 1  Charles  S.  Peters  and  Drummond  Jones  lifted  the  two  large 
cups  offered  by  the  club  to  the  team  that  would  win  the  doubles  cham- 
pionship three  times,  not  necessarily  in  succession.  The  club  has  made 
arrangement  to    have   them    replaced   ft»r   this   year's    competition. 


SPALDING'S    LAWN    TENNIS    ANNUAL.  85 

Jack  Cannon  of  Kansas  City,  an  all-round  athlete,  captured  the  singles 
championship,  and  he  had  to  play  the  very  best  kind  of  tennis  every  day 
to  win  out  in  the  finals  to  defeat  Roland  M.  Hoerr,  one  of  the  Triple  A 
young  stars   who   is  favorably   known   in   the   East  as  a  coming   player. 

The  big  consolation  cup  in  the  singles  was  won  by  Tyson  Dines,  Jr., 
while  the  Serrano  brothers  annexed  the  consolation  cups  in  the  doubles.

Southern  Championship. 


Connie  B.  Doyle  of  Washington  scored  a  double  victory  for  the  second 
time  during  the  week  ending  July  13.  when  he  won  the  singles  champion- 
ship for  the  South  and,  with  his  brother,  H.  E.  Doyle,  captured  the  doubles 
championship  as  well.  The  tournament  was  played  at  Atlanta,  but  did 
not  attract  the  high  class  of  entries  that  it  did  in  past  years.  In  fact, 
there  was  no  one  in  the  event  who  might  be  considered  a  serious  con 
tender  for  the  title  worn  by  the  Washingtonian.  C.  E.  Smith  defeated 
E.  V.  Carter,  Jr.,  in  the  final  round  in  straight  sets,  6/3,  9/7.  Against 
Doyle.  Smith  could  make  but  little  headway.  Beyond  a  very  fast  twist 
service,  the  challenger  had  no  stroke  which  bothered  the  Capital  City  man. 


SPALDING'S    LAWN    TENNIS    ANNUAL.  87 

On    the    other    hand.    Doyle    took    the    net,    effectively    smothered    Smith's 
returns  at   ease,   and   won,   6/2,   6/4,   6/2. 

The  doubles  event  was  won  by  the  Doyles  hands  down.  Not  a  team  in 
the  field  was  able  to  take  a  set  from  the  brothers,  who  disposed  of  B.  M. 
Grant  and  Carter  in  the  final   round,  6/1,  6/3,   6/2.     The  summaries:

Pacific  Northwest  Championship. 

The  twenty-first  annual  tournament  for  the  championship  of  the  Pacific 
Northwest  was  held  on  the  grounds  of  the  Tacoma  Lawn  Tennis  Club, 
Tacoma,  Wash.,  August  7-12,  It  was  by  far  the  biggest  event  of  its  kind 
ever  held  in  that  section,  and  included  Miss  May  Sutton  and  Miss  Mary 
Brown  of  California,  and  the  ex-western  champion,  Nat  Emerson,  now 
residing  in  North  Yakima,  Wash.  In  the  men's  singles,  Emerson  reached 
the  final  round  with  greatest  ease  and  Joe  C.  Tyler  of  Spokane  came 
through  the  other  side,  but  not  without  w-eathering  a  number  of  harrt 
matches.  In  the  final  the  match  between  Emerson  and  Tyler  was  quite 
close  and  very  interesting.  It  lasted  four  sets,  during  which  the  old 
Western  title   holder  held  a  safe   lead.     He  won  at  6/4.   6/3.   3/6.  7/5. 

Emerson  won  the  doubles  with  Russell.  They  defeated  Captain  Foulkes 
and  Tyler  in  the  final  round  in  the  closest  kind  of  a  five-set  match.  This 
set  was  a  battle  royal  until  5-all  was  called,  at  which  point  Tyler  weakened 
and    Russell    rallied    sufficiently   to   land   the   two   necessary   games. 

Of  course  Miss  May  Sutton  won  the  women's  singles,  her  victim  in  the 
final  being  Miss  Brown,  who  was  lucky  to  score  the  single  game  in  each 
set  which  was  her  lot.  Miss  Sutton  and  Captain  Foulkes  won  the  mixed 
doubles.      The    summaries:

Western  New  York  Championship. 

N.  W.  Niles  again  successfully  defended  his  title  of  Western  New  York 
State  champion  at  Buffalo,  where  he  defeated  Charles  Benton  of  Cleve- 
land, Ohio,  who  won  the  tournament  and  the  right  to  challenge  him,  in 
a  decisive  and  somewhat  one-sided  three-set  match.  Benton  had  won  from 
F.  H.  Harris  of  Dartmouth  in  a  five-set  match  in  the  final.  Benton  and 
Harris  took  the  doubles,  getting  a  default  in  the  final  round  from  Hamlin 
and    Russell. 

The  ladies'  singles  was  won  by  Miss  May  Sutton,  who  defeated  her 
sister.  Miss  Florence,  in  the  final  and  then  took  the  title  from  Miss  Lois 
Moyes  of  Toronto,  Canada.  In  neither  match  was  she  extended  to  the 
limit,  winning  with  fair  ease.  She  also  took  the  mixed  doubles  with 
Kirkover,  defeating  Miss  Florence  Sutton  and  Harris  in  the  final  round, 
9/7.   9/7-      The   summaries:

South  Atlantic  Championship. 

On  June  26,  at  the  Country  Club  of  Augusta,  Ga.,  the  South  Atlantic 
tournament  began.  Both  in  class  and  in  number  the  entry  for  this  meet- 
ing was  by  far  the  best  of  any  of  those  that  had  been  previously  held. 
E.  V.  Carter,  Jr..  won  the  event.  Not  one  of  his  opponents  was  able  to 
score  a  set  against  him.  In  the  challenge  round  C.  E.  Ramspeck.  the 
holder,  fared  no  better  than  the  rest  of  Carter's  opponents  and  his  title 
went  glimmering  when  Carter  won  at  7/5.  k/2.  The  latter,  paired  with 
M.  DuBose  of  Athens,  Ga.,  won  the  doubles  championship,  defeating 
Ramspeck  and  Orr  in  the  final  round  at  6/3,  6/4,  3/6,  2/6,  6/4.  The 
summaries:

Northeastern  Pennsylvania  Championship 

The  courts  of  the  Country  Club  of  Scranton  were  again  the  scene  of 
the  Northeastern  Pennsylvania  championship,  which  Tjegan  on  September 
13.  It  resulted  in  the  winning  of  an  open  event  for  the  first  time  by 
little  Leonard  Beekman  of  Bronxville,  N,  Y.,  a  100  pounder  of  sixteen 
years,  who  shows  more  ability  at  his  age  than  most  youngsters  older  than 
he  and  twice  as  heavy.  Beekman  played  splendidly  on  his  way  to  the 
finals,    where   he   met    P.    S.    Osborn.      The    latter    got   away    with    two    sets 


94  SPALDIXGS   LAWN    TENNIS    ANNUAL. 

and  seemed  to  have  the  match  in  hand,  when  Beekman  started  in  with  a 
rush  and  quickly  ended  the  scoring  by  taking  the  third  and  fourth  at  6/1 
each.  The  fifth  was  very  close.  At  4-all  Beekman  broke  through  and  won 
the  next  game  and  match  on  his  own  service.  The  doubles  were  won  by 
the  Markle  brothers  from  McClave  and  Brooks  in  straight  sets.  The 
summaries:

Missouri  Valley  Championsliips 

The  annual  Missouri  \'alley  tennis  championships,  held  under  the  aus- 
pices of  the  Kansas  City  Athletic  Club  at  Kansas  City,  Mo.,  were  perhaps 
the  most  successful  during  the  season  of  .1911  than  at  any  time  during  the 
past  ten  years  or  so  that  the  events  have  been  in  existence. 

The  men's  tournament  was  the  tenth  annual  event  of  its  kind  and  it 
attracted  one  of  the  largest  and  classiest  entries  in  its  history.  Of  the 
prominent  visitors  might  be  named  J.  H.  Winston  and  Hugh  G.  Whitehead, 
Norfolk,  Va.;  Paul  D.  and  Harvey  M.  MacQuiston,  New  Orleans;  R.  M. 
Hoerr  and  W.  C.  Brodix,  St.  Louis;  Ralph  R.  Rainey  and  L.  G.  McConnell, 
Omaha.  The  Kansas  City  Athletic  Club  was  well  represented  by  Jack 
Cannon,  Herbert  V.  Jones,  A.  G.  Welhener,  Harry  W.  Durall  and  a  num- 
ber of  lesser  lights.  There  were  seventy-two  entries  in  the  singles  and 
over  twenty  teams  in  the  doubles. 

Some  splendid  exhibitions  of  tennis  were  in  evidence  during  the  week's 
play  and  when  Friday  found  the  singles  narrowed  down  to  the  semi-finals 
a  rather  curious  feature  presented  itself,  as  the  four  men  left  in  the  tour- 
ney were  the  two  Virginians  and  Cannon  and  Jones  of  Kansas  City.  Can- 
non was  pitted  against  Whitehead  and  Jones  was  slated  to  play  Winston. 
The  first  named  opened  the  afternoon's  play,  Whitehead  forcing  the  pace 
and  taking  the  first  set,  6/2,  although  not  easily.  The  next  was  also  a 
fight  to  the  finish,  but  Cannon  was  equal  to  the  occasion  and  took  the  set, 
7/5,  after  a  desperate  struggle.  The  third  set  was  all  to  the  Kansas  City 
man,  who  seemed  to  have  his  opponent's  measure,  winning,  6/1,  and  the 
match. 

The  Jones-Winston  match  was  even  more  exciting  and  it  looked  several 
times  as  if  the  Kansas  City  A.C.  man  would  win.  He  started  off  with  a 
rush  and  ran  up  the  first  three  games,  only  to  lose  the  set,  6/4.  Again, 
in  the  second  set,  he  set  the  pace  and  had  the  long  Virginian,  5/1,  only 
to   lose  after  a   heart-breaking  struggle,   7/5. 

This  placed  Cannon  vs.  Winston,  and  a  crowd  of  over  one  thousand  was 
not  disappointed  by  any  means,  although  their  champion  was  defeated.  The 
first  set  was  a  serving  match,  each  player  winning  his  service  up  to  9-all. 
Here  Winston  broke  through  and  then  taking  his  own  serve  won  the  set, 
1 1/9.  This  seemed  to  give  him  confidence  and  he  captured  the  second, 
6/1.  Cannon,  however,  is  a  "Marathoner"  and  his  admirers  were  not 
surprised  to  see  him  go  after  the  third  set  in  earnest.  He  ran  the  score 
to  4/2,  and  although  in  the  next  few  games  he  was  several  times  within 
one  point  of  the  game,  he  could  not  get  the  needed  one  and  Winston 
fi.nally    proved    himself   the    winner,    6/4. 

The  doubles  were  won  by  Winston  and  Whitehead  from  Hoerr  and 
Brodix   of   St.   Louis  in   straight  sets,   6/3,   6/4,   6/2. 

The  women's  tournament,  held  the  week  of  July  3,  was  by  far  the  most 
successful  of  its  kind  ever  played  here.  There  were  over  forty  entries  in 
the  singles,  while  sixteen  teams  competed  for  the  doubles  title.  There  was 
decided  interest  taken  in  the  tournaments  and  the  finals  brought  out  an 
audience  of  considerably  over  one  thousand.  Mrs.  Theodore  B.  Entz  of 
St.  Louis,  who  won  the  tournament,  met  a  worthy  opponent  in  Miss  Evelyn 
Seavey  of  Kansas  City,  who  was  the  winner  of  the  previous  year.  The 
match  was  the  hardest  fought  of  the  tournament  and  it  was  perhaps  gen- 
eralship and  experience  that  won  for  the  visitor  more  than  actually  better 
playing,  as  the  scores  were  7/5,  8/6.  Miss  Evelyn  Seavey  and  Miss  Burmah 
Brokaw  won  the  doubles  event  from  Miss  Patience  Hocker  and  Miss  Janet 
McCrurn    by   scores    of  6/3,    11/9. 

Mixed    doubles    were    attempted    for    the    first    time    in    Kansas    City    the 


1    Roland  Hoerr.  Triple  A  Club,  St.  Louis,  Mo.:    2,  Jack  Cannon,   Kansas  City
